#### 3.3p Scattering Amplitude

The scattering amplitude, denoted as $A_{scattered}$, is a fundamental concept in the study of scattering phenomena. It is a measure of the amplitude of the scattered wave, which is a crucial parameter in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

The scattering amplitude is defined as the ratio of the scattered wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
A_{scattered} = \frac{A_{scattered}}{A_{incident}}
$$

where $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave and $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave.

In the context of acoustics, the scattering amplitude plays a crucial role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The scattering amplitude can be calculated using the Mie scattering equation, which we introduced in the previous section. The scattering amplitude provides a measure of the amplitude of the scattered sound wave, which is essential in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The scattering amplitude can also be used to calculate the scattering phase, which is a measure of the phase shift of the scattered wave. The scattering phase can be expressed as:

$$
\delta = \arg\left(\frac{A_{scattered}}{A_{incident}}\right)
$$

where $\delta$ is the scattering phase.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of the scattering amplitude and explore its implications for the study of acoustics in the ocean.

#### 3.3q Scattering Phase

The scattering phase, denoted as $\delta$, is a fundamental concept in the study of scattering phenomena. It is a measure of the phase shift of the scattered wave, which is a crucial parameter in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

The scattering phase is defined as the phase difference between the scattered wave and the incident wave. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
\delta = \arg\left(\frac{A_{scattered}}{A_{incident}}\right)
$$

where $\delta$ is the scattering phase, $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave, and $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave.

In the context of acoustics, the scattering phase plays a crucial role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The scattering phase can be calculated using the Mie scattering equation, which we introduced in the previous section. The scattering phase provides a measure of the phase shift of the scattered sound wave, which is essential in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The scattering phase can also be used to calculate the scattering amplitude, which is a measure of the amplitude of the scattered wave. The scattering amplitude can be expressed as:

$$
A_{scattered} = A_{incident} e^{i\delta}
$$

where $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave, $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave, and $i$ is the imaginary unit.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of the scattering phase and explore its implications for the study of acoustics in the ocean.

#### 3.3r Scattering Matrix

The scattering matrix, denoted as $S$, is a crucial concept in the study of scattering phenomena. It is a matrix representation of the scattering process, which is a crucial parameter in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

The scattering matrix is defined as the ratio of the scattered wave matrix to the incident wave matrix. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
S = \frac{S_{scattered}}{S_{incident}}
$$

where $S_{scattered}$ is the matrix of the scattered wave and $S_{incident}$ is the matrix of the incident wave.

In the context of acoustics, the scattering matrix plays a crucial role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The scattering matrix can be calculated using the Mie scattering equation, which we introduced in the previous section. The scattering matrix provides a measure of the scattering process of the sound wave, which is essential in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The scattering matrix can also be used to calculate the scattering amplitude and phase, which are measures of the amplitude and phase shift of the scattered wave. The scattering amplitude and phase can be expressed as:

$$
A_{scattered} = S_{scattered} A_{incident}
$$

$$
\delta = \arg\left(S_{scattered} A_{incident}\right)
$$

where $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave, $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave, and $\delta$ is the scattering phase.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of the scattering matrix and explore its implications for the study of acoustics in the ocean.

#### 3.3s Scattering Coefficient

The scattering coefficient, denoted as $b$, is a fundamental concept in the study of scattering phenomena. It is a measure of the scattering cross-section per unit volume, which is a crucial parameter in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

The scattering coefficient is defined as the ratio of the scattering cross-section to the volume of the scattering medium. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
b = \frac{\sigma}{V}
$$

where $\sigma$ is the scattering cross-section and $V$ is the volume of the scattering medium.

In the context of acoustics, the scattering coefficient plays a crucial role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The scattering coefficient can be calculated using the Mie scattering equation, which we introduced in the previous section. The scattering coefficient provides a measure of the scattering process of the sound wave per unit volume, which is essential in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The scattering coefficient can also be used to calculate the scattering amplitude and phase, which are measures of the amplitude and phase shift of the scattered wave. The scattering amplitude and phase can be expressed as:

$$
A_{scattered} = b A_{incident}
$$

$$
\delta = \arg\left(b A_{incident}\right)
$$

where $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave, $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave, and $\delta$ is the scattering phase.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of the scattering coefficient and explore its implications for the study of acoustics in the ocean.

#### 3.3t Scattering Cross-Section

The scattering cross-section, denoted as $\sigma$, is a fundamental concept in the study of scattering phenomena. It is a measure of the effective area of a scattering medium that interacts with an incident wave. The larger the scattering cross-section, the more effective the scattering medium is in scattering the wave.

The scattering cross-section is defined as the area of a sphere that intercepts the same volume as the scattering medium. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
\sigma = \pi r^2
$$

where $r$ is the radius of the sphere.

In the context of acoustics, the scattering cross-section plays a crucial role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. The scattering cross-section can be calculated using the Mie scattering equation, which we introduced in the previous section. The scattering cross-section provides a measure of the scattering process of the sound wave, which is essential in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The scattering cross-section can also be used to calculate the scattering amplitude and phase, which are measures of the amplitude and phase shift of the scattered wave. The scattering amplitude and phase can be expressed as:

$$
A_{scattered} = \sigma A_{incident}
$$

$$
\delta = \arg\left(\sigma A_{incident}\right)
$$

where $A_{scattered}$ is the amplitude of the scattered wave, $A_{incident}$ is the amplitude of the incident wave, and $\delta$ is the scattering phase.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of the scattering cross-section and explore its implications for the study of acoustics in the ocean.

#### 3.4a Absorption Coefficients

Absorption coefficients are a crucial concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. They describe the amount of sound energy that is absorbed by the water as it propagates through the medium. The absorption coefficient is typically denoted by the symbol $\alpha$, and it is defined as the fraction of sound energy that is absorbed per unit length of propagation.

The absorption coefficient is a function of the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. It is typically measured in decibels per meter (dB/m).

The absorption coefficient can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \alpha_0 \cdot \left(\frac{f}{f_0}\right)^n \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^m \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^l
$$

where $\alpha_0$ is the absorption coefficient at a reference frequency $f_0$, temperature $T_0$, and salinity $S_0$. The exponents $n$, $m$, and $l$ are empirical constants that describe the frequency, temperature, and salinity dependence of the absorption coefficient, respectively.

The absorption coefficient is a key parameter in the calculation of the sound speed in water, as it is used in the equation of state for sound in water. The equation of state for sound in water is given by:

$$
c = c_0 \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^{-\beta} \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^{\gamma} \cdot e^{\alpha \cdot z}
$$

where $c$ is the sound speed, $c_0$ is the sound speed at a reference temperature $T_0$ and salinity $S_0$, $\beta$ and $\gamma$ are the thermal and haline inversive indices, respectively, and $z$ is the depth.

The absorption coefficient is also used in the calculation of the attenuation of sound in water. The attenuation of sound in water is given by:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \alpha \cdot \int_{0}^{z} c \cdot dz
$$

where $A$ is the attenuation, and the integral is taken over the depth of the water column.

In the next section, we will discuss the factors that influence the absorption coefficient, and how it can be measured in the ocean.

#### 3.4b Absorption in Water

The absorption of sound in water is a complex process that involves the interaction of sound waves with the molecules of the water. This interaction is primarily due to the viscosity of the water, which causes the sound waves to lose energy as they propagate through the medium.

The absorption of sound in water can be described by the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \alpha_0 \cdot \left(\frac{f}{f_0}\right)^n \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^m \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^l
$$

where $\alpha_0$ is the absorption coefficient at a reference frequency $f_0$, temperature $T_0$, and salinity $S_0$. The exponents $n$, $m$, and $l$ are empirical constants that describe the frequency, temperature, and salinity dependence of the absorption coefficient, respectively.

The absorption coefficient is a key parameter in the calculation of the sound speed in water, as it is used in the equation of state for sound in water. The equation of state for sound in water is given by:

$$
c = c_0 \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^{-\beta} \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^{\gamma} \cdot e^{\alpha \cdot z}
$$

where $c$ is the sound speed, $c_0$ is the sound speed at a reference temperature $T_0$ and salinity $S_0$, $\beta$ and $\gamma$ are the thermal and haline inversive indices, respectively, and $z$ is the depth.

The absorption of sound in water is also influenced by the presence of dissolved gases, particularly oxygen and carbon dioxide. These gases can absorb sound energy, leading to an increase in the absorption coefficient. This effect is particularly pronounced at low frequencies, where the absorption coefficient can increase by several decibels per meter.

In addition to these factors, the absorption of sound in water can also be influenced by the presence of particles, such as plankton and sediment. These particles can scatter sound waves, leading to an increase in the absorption coefficient.

In the next section, we will discuss the methods used to measure the absorption of sound in water, and how these measurements can be used to study the properties of the ocean.

#### 3.4c Attenuation in Water

Attenuation is a critical concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the reduction in sound intensity as the sound wave propagates through the water. This reduction in intensity is primarily due to absorption, but it can also be influenced by other factors such as scattering and reflection.

The attenuation of sound in water can be described by the following equation:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \alpha \cdot \int_{0}^{z} c \cdot dz
$$

where $A$ is the attenuation, $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, $c$ is the sound speed, and $z$ is the depth. The integral is taken over the depth of the water column.

The attenuation coefficient, denoted by $\beta$, is a key parameter in the calculation of the sound speed in water. It is used in the equation of state for sound in water, which is given by:

$$
c = c_0 \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^{-\beta} \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^{\gamma} \cdot e^{\alpha \cdot z}
$$

where $c_0$ is the sound speed at a reference temperature $T_0$ and salinity $S_0$, $\beta$ and $\gamma$ are the thermal and haline inversive indices, respectively, and $z$ is the depth.

The attenuation coefficient is a function of the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. It is typically measured in decibels per meter (dB/m).

The attenuation of sound in water can be influenced by several factors. These include the presence of dissolved gases, particularly oxygen and carbon dioxide, which can absorb sound energy and increase the attenuation coefficient. The presence of particles, such as plankton and sediment, can also increase the attenuation coefficient by scattering and absorbing sound waves.

In addition to these factors, the attenuation of sound in water can also be influenced by the properties of the water itself. For example, the attenuation coefficient can increase with increasing temperature and salinity, due to the increased viscosity and density of the water.

In the next section, we will discuss the methods used to measure the attenuation of sound in water, and how these measurements can be used to study the properties of the ocean.

#### 3.4d Scattering in Water

Scattering is another important phenomenon in underwater sound propagation. It refers to the redirection of sound waves from their original path due to interaction with particles in the water. These particles can be of various sizes and densities, and they can be suspended in the water due to turbulence or other physical processes.

The scattering of sound in water can be described by the Rayleigh scattering theory, which is based on the assumption that the particles are much smaller than the wavelength of the sound. The scattering cross-section, denoted by $\sigma$, is given by:

$$
\sigma = \frac{2 \pi r^4 \rho^2}{\lambda^4} \cdot \left(\frac{4 \pi}{\lambda r}\right)^4 \cdot \left(\frac{1 - \cos(\theta)}{\theta}\right)^2
$$

where $r$ is the radius of the particle, $\rho$ is the density of the particle, $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the sound, $\theta$ is the scattering angle, and $\cos(\theta)$ is the cosine of the scattering angle.

The scattering coefficient, denoted by $\beta_s$, is a key parameter in the calculation of the sound speed in water. It is used in the equation of state for sound in water, which is given by:

$$
c = c_0 \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^{-\beta} \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^{\gamma} \cdot e^{\alpha \cdot z}
$$

where $c_0$ is the sound speed at a reference temperature $T_0$ and salinity $S_0$, $\beta$ and $\gamma$ are the thermal and haline inversive indices, respectively, and $z$ is the depth.

The scattering coefficient is a function of the frequency of the sound wave, the temperature and salinity of the water, and the depth of the water column. It is typically measured in decibels per meter (dB/m).

The scattering of sound in water can be influenced by several factors. These include the presence of particles, such as plankton and sediment, which can scatter sound waves and increase the scattering coefficient. The scattering coefficient can also increase with increasing temperature and salinity, due to the increased density and viscosity of the water.

In addition to these factors, the scattering of sound in water can also be influenced by the properties of the particles themselves. For example, the scattering coefficient can increase with increasing particle size and density, due to the increased scattering cross-section.

#### 3.4e Reflection and Refraction

Reflection and refraction are two fundamental processes that occur when sound waves encounter a boundary between two different media. These processes are crucial in understanding the propagation of sound in the ocean, as they can significantly alter the direction and intensity of sound waves.

Reflection occurs when a sound wave encounters a boundary and is bounced back into the original medium. The law of reflection states that the angle of incidence, denoted by $i$, is equal to the angle of reflection, denoted by $r$. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
i = r
$$

where $i$ and $r$ are measured in radians and are positive if the sound wave is traveling in the positive $x$-direction.

Refraction, on the other hand, occurs when a sound wave passes from one medium to another and changes its direction. The law of refraction, also known as Snell's law, states that the ratio of the sine of the angle of incidence to the sine of the angle of refraction is equal to the inverse ratio of the sound speeds in the two media. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
n_1 \cdot \sin(i) = n_2 \cdot \sin(r)
$$

where $n_1$ and $n_2$ are the sound speeds in the first and second medium, respectively, and $i$ and $r$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively.

These processes can be visualized using the concept of the ray diagram, which is a graphical representation of the propagation of sound waves. The ray diagram can be used to calculate the angles of incidence and refraction, and to determine whether a sound wave will be reflected or refracted when it encounters a boundary.

In the context of underwater sound propagation, these processes can be particularly important. For example, they can determine whether a sound wave will be able to penetrate a boundary layer, such as the thermocline or halocline, and how much of the sound energy will be lost due to reflection or refraction.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound channel, which is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering.

#### 3.4f Boundary Layers

Boundary layers are regions of water where the properties of the water, such as its density and sound speed, change rapidly with depth. These layers can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The thermocline and halocline are two important examples of boundary layers. The thermocline is the layer where the temperature of the water changes rapidly with depth, and the halocline is the layer where the salinity of the water changes rapidly with depth.

The properties of the water in these layers can change rapidly enough that they can be considered as discontinuities in the water. This means that when a sound wave encounters one of these layers, it will be reflected or refracted. The amount of reflection or refraction will depend on the difference in properties between the layer and the water above or below it.

The thermocline and halocline can also affect the propagation of sound waves by altering the stratification of the water. Stratification refers to the layering of the water, with denser water sinking below less dense water. Changes in the stratification can affect the speed at which sound waves travel through the water, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

The effects of the thermocline and halocline on sound propagation can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. A sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The thermocline and halocline can create barriers to sound propagation, which can limit the range of sound waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound channel in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4g Sound Channels

Sound channels are regions of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. These channels are created by the stratification of the water, which refers to the layering of the water with denser water sinking below less dense water.

The concept of the sound channel is crucial in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. It allows us to model the effects of the thermocline and halocline on sound propagation, and to predict the range of sound waves in the ocean.

The sound channel can be modeled using the concept of the ray diagram. The ray diagram is a graphical representation of the propagation of sound waves, and can be used to calculate the angles of incidence and refraction. These angles can determine whether a sound wave will be able to penetrate a boundary layer, such as the thermocline or halocline, and how much of the sound energy will be lost due to reflection or refraction.

The sound channel can also be modeled using the concept of the sound speed profile. The sound speed profile is a plot of the sound speed with depth, and can be used to predict the speed at which sound waves will travel through the water. Changes in the sound speed profile can affect the stratification of the water, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed profile in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4h Sound Speed Profiles

Sound speed profiles are graphical representations of the sound speed with depth in the water. These profiles are crucial in understanding the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. They allow us to predict the speed at which sound waves will travel through the water, and to model the effects of the thermocline and halocline on sound propagation.

The sound speed profile is typically represented as a function of depth, with the surface of the water at the top and the seafloor at the bottom. The sound speed is usually plotted in decibels per meter (dB/m), and the depth is usually plotted in meters.

The sound speed profile can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. The sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The sound speed profile can be used to predict the speed at which sound waves will travel through the water, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

The sound speed profile can also be modeled using the concept of the sound speed anomaly. The sound speed anomaly is the difference between the actual sound speed and the sound speed predicted by the sound speed profile. The sound speed anomaly can be caused by changes in the properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density.

The sound speed profile can be calculated using the equation of state for sound in water. This equation relates the sound speed to the temperature, salinity, and depth of the water. It can be written as:

$$
c = c_0 \cdot \left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)^{-\beta} \cdot \left(\frac{S}{S_0}\right)^{\gamma} \cdot e^{\alpha \cdot z}
$$

where $c$ is the sound speed, $c_0$ is the sound speed at a reference temperature and salinity, $T$ and $S$ are the temperature and salinity of the water, $\beta$ and $\gamma$ are the thermal and haline inversive indices, respectively, $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, and $z$ is the depth.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed anomaly in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4i Sound Speed Anomalies

Sound speed anomalies are a critical concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. They represent the difference between the actual sound speed and the sound speed predicted by the sound speed profile. These anomalies can be caused by changes in the properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density.

The sound speed anomaly, denoted as $A$, can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A = c - c_0
$$

where $c$ is the actual sound speed and $c_0$ is the sound speed predicted by the sound speed profile.

The sound speed anomaly can be positive or negative, depending on whether the actual sound speed is higher or lower than the predicted sound speed. Positive anomalies indicate that the sound speed is higher than predicted, while negative anomalies indicate that the sound speed is lower than predicted.

The sound speed anomaly can be influenced by several factors. These include the temperature, salinity, and density of the water, as well as the depth of the water. Changes in these factors can cause the sound speed to deviate from the predicted sound speed, resulting in a sound speed anomaly.

The sound speed anomaly can also be influenced by the presence of sound speed anomalies in the water. These anomalies can interact with each other, leading to complex patterns of sound speed anomalies in the water.

The sound speed anomaly can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. The sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The sound speed anomaly can affect the boundaries of the sound channel, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed anomaly in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4j Sound Speed Variations

Sound speed variations are another important concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. They represent the changes in sound speed with depth in the water. These variations can be caused by changes in the properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density.

The sound speed variation, denoted as $V$, can be calculated using the equation:

$$
V = \frac{dc}{dz}
$$

where $dc$ is the change in sound speed and $dz$ is the change in depth.

The sound speed variation can be positive or negative, depending on whether the sound speed is increasing or decreasing with depth. Positive variations indicate that the sound speed is increasing with depth, while negative variations indicate that the sound speed is decreasing with depth.

The sound speed variation can be influenced by several factors. These include the temperature, salinity, and density of the water, as well as the depth of the water. Changes in these factors can cause the sound speed to change with depth, resulting in a sound speed variation.

The sound speed variation can also be influenced by the presence of sound speed anomalies in the water. These anomalies can interact with each other, leading to complex patterns of sound speed variations in the water.

The sound speed variation can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. The sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The sound speed variation can affect the boundaries of the sound channel, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed variation in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4k Sound Speed Stratification

Sound speed stratification is a critical concept in the study of underwater sound propagation. It refers to the layering of water with different sound speeds, which can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

The sound speed stratification can be visualized as a series of layers, each with its own sound speed. These layers can be created by changes in the properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density. For example, the thermocline, which is the layer where the temperature of the water changes rapidly with depth, can create a sound speed stratification.

The sound speed stratification can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. The sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The sound speed stratification can affect the boundaries of the sound channel, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

The sound speed stratification can be calculated using the equation:

$$
S = \frac{dc}{dz}
$$

where $dc$ is the change in sound speed and $dz$ is the change in depth.

The sound speed stratification can be positive or negative, depending on whether the sound speed is increasing or decreasing with depth. Positive stratification indicates that the sound speed is increasing with depth, while negative stratification indicates that the sound speed is decreasing with depth.

The sound speed stratification can also be influenced by the presence of sound speed anomalies in the water. These anomalies can interact with each other, leading to complex patterns of sound speed stratification in the water.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed stratification in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4l Sound Speed Profiles and Anomalies

Sound speed profiles and anomalies are crucial concepts in the study of underwater sound propagation. They provide a detailed understanding of how sound speed changes with depth in the ocean, which can significantly affect the propagation of sound waves.

A sound speed profile is a graphical representation of the sound speed with depth. It is typically represented as a function of depth, with the surface of the water at the top and the seafloor at the bottom. The sound speed is usually plotted in decibels per meter (dB/m), and the depth is usually plotted in meters.

The sound speed profile can be modeled using the concept of the sound channel. The sound channel is a region of water where sound waves can propagate with minimal loss due to absorption and scattering. The sound speed profile can affect the boundaries of the sound channel, and can also cause sound waves to bend.

The sound speed anomaly, on the other hand, is the difference between the actual sound speed and the sound speed predicted by the sound speed profile. These anomalies can be caused by changes in the properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density. They can also be influenced by the presence of sound speed anomalies in the water.

The sound speed anomaly can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A = c - c_0
$$

where $c$ is the actual sound speed and $c_0$ is the sound speed predicted by the sound speed profile.

The sound speed anomaly can be positive or negative, depending on whether the actual sound speed is higher or lower than the predicted sound speed. Positive anomalies indicate that the sound speed is higher than predicted, while negative anomalies indicate that the sound speed is lower than predicted.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of the sound speed anomaly in more detail, and will explore how it can be used to model the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

#### 3.4b Attenuation Mechanisms

Attenuation is a critical concept in underwater sound propagation. It refers to the reduction in sound intensity as the sound wave propagates through the water. The attenuation of sound in water is primarily due to absorption and scattering.

Absorption is the process by which sound energy is converted into heat as the sound wave propagates through the water. This process is primarily due to the interaction of the sound wave with the molecules of the water. The absorption of sound in water is frequency-dependent, with higher frequencies being absorbed more than lower frequencies. This is due to the fact that higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, and therefore interact more strongly with the molecules of the water.

The absorption of sound in water can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \alpha \cdot \int_{0}^{z} c \cdot dz
$$

where $A$ is the attenuation, $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, $c$ is the sound speed, and $z$ is the depth.

Scattering is the process by which sound energy is redirected from its original direction as the sound wave propagates through the water. This process is primarily due to the interaction of the sound wave with particles in the water, such as plankton, bubbles, and sediment. The scattering of sound in water is also frequency-dependent, with higher frequencies being scattered more than lower frequencies. This is due to the fact that higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, and therefore interact more strongly with the particles in the water.

The scattering of sound in water can be calculated using the equation:

$$
S = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \beta \cdot \int_{0}^{z} c \cdot dz
$$

where $S$ is the scattering, $\beta$ is the scattering coefficient, $c$ is the sound speed, and $z$ is the depth.

The total attenuation of sound in water is the sum of the absorption and scattering attenuations. This can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A_{total} = A + S
$$

where $A_{total}$ is the total attenuation.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of these attenuation mechanisms on underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.4c Absorption and Scattering Models

In the previous section, we discussed the basic concepts of absorption and scattering in underwater sound propagation. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical models that describe these phenomena.

The absorption of sound in water can be modeled using the Kolsky basic model and modified model for attenuation and dispersion. The Kolsky basic model is given by:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \omega \cdot \left(\frac{1}{\delta_1} + \frac{1}{\delta_2}\right)
$$

where $\alpha$ is the absorption coefficient, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, and $\delta_1$ and $\delta_2$ are the phase velocities of the sound wave in the water.

The modified model for attenuation and dispersion is given by:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \omega \cdot \left(\frac{1}{\delta_1} + \frac{1}{\delta_2}\right) + \frac{1}{2} \cdot \omega \cdot \left(\frac{1}{\delta_1} - \frac{1}{\delta_2}\right) \cdot \left(\frac{1}{\delta_1} + \frac{1}{\delta_2}\right)^{-1}
$$

The first term in this equation represents the absorption of sound, while the second term represents the dispersion of sound.

The scattering of sound in water can be modeled using the non-radiative dielectric waveguide model. This model is given by:

$$
T_{o-}^{TE} = H \cdot cos\left(\frac{m \cdot \pi}{a} \cdot y\right) \cdot e^{jk_xo \cdot (x-w)}
$$

where $T_{o-}^{TE}$ is the transverse electric field, $H$ is the height of the waveguide, $m$ is the mode number, $a$ is the width of the waveguide, $y$ is the vertical coordinate, $k_xo$ is the wave number in the x-direction, $x$ is the horizontal coordinate, and $w$ is the width of the waveguide.

These models provide a mathematical framework for understanding the absorption and scattering of sound in water. However, it is important to note that these models are simplifications of the complex physical processes that occur in the ocean. Therefore, they should be used with caution and their results should be interpreted in the context of the assumptions and limitations of the models.

#### 3.4d Absorption and Scattering Measurements

In the previous section, we discussed the mathematical models that describe the absorption and scattering of sound in water. In this section, we will explore the practical aspects of measuring these phenomena in the ocean.

The absorption of sound in water can be measured using various techniques, including the use of acoustic tomography and the measurement of sound intensity. Acoustic tomography is a technique that uses the transmission of sound waves to create a picture of the ocean. This technique can be used to measure the absorption of sound in water over large areas.

The absorption of sound can also be measured directly by measuring the sound intensity. This can be done using a hydrophone, which is a device that can detect and measure sound in water. The sound intensity can be measured at different depths and frequencies to determine the absorption coefficient.

The scattering of sound in water can be measured using techniques such as backscattering and side scattering. Backscattering is the reflection of sound waves back in the direction from which they came. Side scattering is the deflection of sound waves to the side. These phenomena can be measured using a scatterometer, which is a device that can detect and measure the scattering of sound in water.

The scattering of sound can also be measured directly by measuring the sound intensity. This can be done using a hydrophone, which can detect and measure the sound intensity at different angles. The sound intensity can be measured at different depths and frequencies to determine the scattering coefficient.

It is important to note that these measurements are influenced by various factors, including the properties of the water, the presence of particles and bubbles, and the frequency of the sound. Therefore, it is crucial to take these factors into account when interpreting the results of these measurements.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of absorption and scattering on underwater sound propagation.

#### 3.4e Absorption and Scattering Applications

In this section, we will explore some of the practical applications of absorption and scattering in underwater sound propagation. These applications are crucial in various fields, including oceanography, acoustics, and telecommunications.

One of the primary applications of absorption and scattering is in the field of acoustics. The absorption and scattering of sound in water play a significant role in the propagation of sound waves in the ocean. This knowledge is crucial in the design and operation of underwater acoustic devices, such as sonar and underwater microphones.

For instance, the absorption of sound in water can be used to estimate the depth of the ocean. This is done by measuring the sound intensity at different depths and frequencies. The absorption coefficient can then be used to estimate the depth of the ocean. This technique is particularly useful in deep ocean environments where other depth estimation methods may not be feasible.

The scattering of sound in water is also important in acoustics. It is used in the design of sonar systems, which are used to detect and locate objects underwater. The scattering of sound can be used to determine the direction and distance of objects underwater. This is done by measuring the backscattering and side scattering of sound waves.

In the field of telecommunications, absorption and scattering are used in the design of underwater communication systems. These systems use sound waves to transmit information underwater. The absorption and scattering of sound can affect the quality of the transmitted signal. Therefore, understanding these phenomena is crucial in the design and operation of these systems.

In addition to these applications, absorption and scattering are also used in oceanography. They are used to study the properties of the ocean, such as its depth, temperature, and salinity. This is done by measuring the absorption and scattering of sound waves in the ocean.

In conclusion, absorption and scattering play a crucial role in underwater sound propagation. They have a wide range of applications in various fields, including acoustics, telecommunications, and oceanography. Understanding these phenomena is crucial for the design and operation of various devices and systems.

This interaction can be influenced by a variety of factors, including the properties of the ocean's medium, the frequency of the sound, and the depth of the water. We have also learned that sound propagation in the ocean can be affected by various phenomena, such as refraction, reflection, and absorption.

We have also discussed the importance of understanding underwater sound propagation in various fields, such as oceanography, acoustics, and marine engineering. Understanding sound propagation in the ocean can help us to better understand the ocean's environment, to design more effective acoustic devices, and to improve the safety and efficiency of marine operations.

In conclusion, the study of underwater sound propagation is a fascinating and important field that has many practical applications. By understanding the principles and phenomena that govern sound propagation in the ocean, we can gain a deeper understanding of the ocean's environment and improve our ability to use the ocean for various purposes.

## Chapter 4: Reverberation

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex environment, teeming with life and filled with sound. The study of this sound, known as acoustics, is a crucial aspect of oceanography. In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of reverberation, a key concept in underwater acoustics.

Reverberation is the reflection and redirection of sound waves in an enclosed space. In the context of the ocean, it refers to the reflection of sound waves off the seafloor, water column, and surface. This phenomenon plays a significant role in the propagation of sound in the ocean, influencing everything from the transmission of information to the detection of objects.

Understanding reverberation is not just about knowing how sound behaves in the ocean. It's about understanding the ocean itself. The ocean is not a perfect conductor of sound, nor is it a perfect reflector. It's a complex medium that absorbs, reflects, and redirects sound in intricate ways. By studying reverberation, we can gain insights into the physical properties of the ocean, such as its depth, composition, and temperature.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of reverberation, its effects on sound propagation, and its applications in oceanography. We will also discuss the mathematical models used to describe reverberation, such as the impulse response and the reverberation time. These models, expressed in terms of the Schrödinger equation, provide a quantitative framework for understanding and predicting reverberation.

As we journey through this chapter, we will also touch upon the practical implications of reverberation. For instance, how does reverberation affect the detection of submarines and marine life? How can we use reverberation to map the seafloor? These are just some of the questions we will explore.

So, let's dive into the world of reverberation and discover the ocean's hidden acoustic landscape.




#### 3.4c Frequency and Depth Dependence

The absorption and scattering of sound in water are not constant across all frequencies and depths. The absorption and scattering coefficients, $\alpha$ and $\beta$, respectively, vary with frequency and depth. This variation is due to the physical properties of the water, such as its temperature, salinity, and density, which affect the interaction of sound waves with the water.

The absorption coefficient, $\alpha$, increases with frequency and depth. This is because higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, and therefore interact more strongly with the molecules of the water, leading to increased absorption. Additionally, the absorption coefficient increases with depth due to the increasing pressure, which also increases the interaction between the sound wave and the water molecules.

The scattering coefficient, $\beta$, also increases with frequency and depth. This is because higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, and therefore interact more strongly with the particles in the water, leading to increased scattering. Additionally, the scattering coefficient increases with depth due to the increasing concentration of particles in the water, such as plankton, bubbles, and sediment.

The variation of the absorption and scattering coefficients with frequency and depth can be described by the following equations:

$$
\alpha = \alpha_0 \cdot f^a \cdot e^{b \cdot z}
$$

$$
\beta = \beta_0 \cdot f^b \cdot e^{c \cdot z}
$$

where $\alpha_0$ and $\beta_0$ are the absorption and scattering coefficients at a reference frequency and depth, respectively, $f$ is the frequency, $a$ and $b$ are the frequency exponents, $z$ is the depth, and $b$ and $c$ are the depth exponents.

The frequency and depth dependence of the absorption and scattering coefficients have significant implications for underwater sound propagation. For example, it affects the range and direction of sound propagation, as well as the attenuation of sound with distance. Understanding these dependencies is crucial for predicting and modeling sound propagation in the ocean.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of underwater sound propagation. We have learned about the unique properties of sound in water, including its speed, wavelength, and attenuation. We have also delved into the various factors that affect sound propagation, such as temperature, salinity, and pressure. Additionally, we have discussed the different types of sound sources and receivers used in acoustical oceanography, and how they are used to study the ocean.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding sound propagation in the ocean. Sound plays a crucial role in many aspects of marine life, from communication and navigation to detecting prey and predators. By studying sound propagation, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior and interactions of marine organisms. Furthermore, understanding sound propagation is essential for developing effective methods of underwater communication and navigation.

As we continue to explore the depths of sound in the ocean, it is important to keep in mind the potential impact of human activities on underwater sound. With the increasing use of sonar and other sound-producing technologies, it is crucial to understand how sound travels in the ocean and how it affects marine life. By studying underwater sound propagation, we can work towards developing more sustainable and responsible practices for using sound in the ocean.

## Chapter: Acoustical Oceanography: Exploring the Depths of Sound in the Ocean

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of underwater acoustics. Acoustics is the study of sound and its behavior in different mediums, and underwater acoustics is a specialized field that focuses on sound in the ocean. The ocean is a vast and complex environment, and understanding how sound behaves in this medium is crucial for a variety of applications, including navigation, communication, and studying marine life.

We will begin by exploring the basic principles of sound, including its properties and behavior in different mediums. We will then delve into the unique characteristics of sound in the ocean, such as its speed, wavelength, and attenuation. We will also discuss the various factors that affect sound in the ocean, such as temperature, salinity, and pressure.

Next, we will explore the different types of underwater sound sources and receivers, including active and passive systems. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of using sound in the ocean, such as interference and noise.

Finally, we will examine some of the applications of underwater acoustics, such as sonar, acoustic tomography, and acoustic tagging. We will also discuss the future of underwater acoustics and the potential for new advancements in this field.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of underwater acoustics and its importance in the study of the ocean. So let's dive in and explore the depths of sound in the ocean.

The use of acoustic signals has become increasingly important in oceanography, as it allows us to study the ocean in a non-invasive and cost-effective manner.

### Section: 4.1 Time-Frequency Analysis

Time-frequency analysis is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography that allows us to study the behavior of acoustic signals over time and frequency. It is particularly useful in understanding the complex nature of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and spectral variations.

#### 4.1a Fourier Transform and Spectrogram

The Fourier Transform is a mathematical tool that decomposes a signal into its constituent frequencies. In the context of acoustical oceanography, it is often used to analyze the spectral content of acoustic signals. The Fourier Transform is defined as:

$$
F(\omega) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)e^{-j\omega t} dt
$$

where $x(t)$ is the signal of interest, $\omega$ is the frequency, and $j$ is the imaginary unit. The Fourier Transform provides a frequency domain representation of the signal, where each frequency component is represented by a complex number.

The Spectrogram, on the other hand, is a time-frequency representation of a signal. It provides a visual representation of the spectral content of a signal over time. The Spectrogram is computed by applying a series of Fourier Transforms to the signal at different time intervals. The result is a two-dimensional plot where the horizontal axis represents time and the vertical axis represents frequency. The intensity of the plot at each point represents the power of the signal at that particular time and frequency.

The Spectrogram is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography as it allows us to visualize the changes in the spectral content of acoustic signals over time. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and spectral variations due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

In the next section, we will explore the implementation of the Fourier Transform and Spectrogram in MATLAB, and discuss their applications in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1b Wavelet Transform and Wavelet Spectrum

The Wavelet Transform is another powerful tool in time-frequency analysis. Unlike the Fourier Transform, which provides a frequency domain representation of a signal, the Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation. This is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where we often need to analyze the temporal variations of acoustic signals.

The Wavelet Transform is defined as:

$$
W(a, b) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{|a|}} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t) \psi^* \left(\frac{t - b}{a}\right) dt
$$

where $x(t)$ is the signal of interest, $\psi(t)$ is the wavelet function, $a$ is the scale parameter, and $b$ is the translation parameter. The Wavelet Transform provides a time-scale representation of the signal, where each scale component is represented by a complex number.

The Wavelet Spectrum, similar to the Spectrogram, is a time-frequency representation of a signal. However, unlike the Spectrogram, which provides a frequency domain representation, the Wavelet Spectrum provides a time-scale representation. The Wavelet Spectrum is computed by applying a series of Wavelet Transforms to the signal at different scales. The result is a two-dimensional plot where the horizontal axis represents time and the vertical axis represents scale. The intensity of the plot at each point represents the power of the signal at that particular time and scale.

The Wavelet Spectrum is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography as it allows us to visualize the changes in the temporal variations of acoustic signals over scale. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and scale variations due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

In the next section, we will explore the implementation of the Wavelet Transform and Wavelet Spectrum in MATLAB, and discuss their applications in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1c Least-Squares Spectral Analysis

The Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA) is a method used to estimate the power spectrum of a signal. It is based on the principle of least squares, which minimizes the sum of the squares of the residuals (the difference between the observed and predicted values). The LSSA is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where we often need to estimate the power spectrum of acoustic signals.

The LSSA involves computing the least-squares spectrum by performing the least-squares approximation multiple times, each time to get the spectral power for a different frequency. This is done by computing "m" spectral values, where "m" is the desired number of frequencies. For each frequency, sine and cosine functions are evaluated at the times corresponding to the data samples, and dot products of the data vector with the sinusoid vectors are taken and appropriately normalized.

The LSSA treats each sinusoidal component independently, even though they may not be orthogonal to data points. This is in contrast to the Lomb/Scargle periodogram method, which can use an arbitrarily high number of, or density of, frequency components, as in a standard periodogram. However, the LSSA cannot fit more components (sines and cosines) than there are data samples, which is a limitation that the Lomb/Scargle periodogram method does not have.

The LSSA can be implemented in MATLAB using the following code:

```
function [P, f] = LSSA(y, fs, m)
    % y is the signal of interest
    % fs is the sampling rate
    % m is the desired number of frequencies

    % Compute the least-squares spectrum
    P = zeros(m, 1);
    for i = 1:m
        t = (0:length(y)-1)/fs; % time vector
        s = sin(2*pi*i/length(y)*t); % sine vector
        c = cos(2*pi*i/length(y)*t); % cosine vector
        r = y'*s; % dot product of data vector with sine vector
        g = y'*c; % dot product of data vector with cosine vector
        P(i) = (r^2 + g^2)/(length(y)*fs); % power at frequency i
    end

    % Compute the frequencies
    f = (0:m-1)*fs/length(y); % frequencies
end
```

The LSSA provides a frequency domain representation of a signal, where each frequency component is represented by a power value. This can be particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where we often need to analyze the spectral content of acoustic signals.

In the next section, we will explore the implementation of the LSSA in MATLAB, and discuss its applications in acoustical oceanography.

Time-frequency analysis allows us to study the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, providing insights into how these properties affect the propagation of sound.

For example, the Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA) can be used to estimate the power spectrum of acoustic signals. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it allows us to estimate the power at different frequencies. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, as it allows us to estimate the power at different frequencies.

Time-frequency analysis allows us to study the behavior of these sound waves, providing insights into how these factors affect their propagation.

In conclusion, time-frequency analysis is a powerful tool in acoustical oceanography, providing valuable insights into the behavior of sound waves in the ocean. #### 4.1b Short-Time Fourier Transform

The Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT) is a variation of the Fourier Transform that allows us to analyze the spectral content of a signal over short periods of time. This is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where the spectral content of acoustic signals can change rapidly over time due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

The STFT is computed by dividing the signal into short segments, and applying a Fourier Transform to each segment. The result is a two-dimensional plot where the horizontal axis represents time and the vertical axis represents frequency. The intensity of the plot at each point represents the power of the signal at that particular time and frequency.

The STFT is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography as it allows us to visualize the changes in the spectral content of acoustic signals over time. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and spectral variations due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

The STFT can be computed using the following formula:

$$
X[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}
$$

where $f[n]$ is the signal of interest, $w[n]$ is a window function, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The STFT can also be computed using the sliding window interpretation, which involves using the discrete Fourier transform (DFT). Let $w_r[n]=w[rL-n]$ be the window element obtained from shifted and flipped window $\mathbf{w}$. Then we have

$$
\mathbf{Y}=[\mathbf{Y}_0,\mathbf{Y}_1...,\mathbf{Y}_{R-1}]
$$

where $\mathbf{Y}_r = \mathbf{x}\circ\mathbf{w}_r$.

The STFT phase retrieval problem can be stated as: Find $\mathbf{x}$ such that $
\mathbf{Z}_{w}[m,r]=|Y[m,r]|^2
$ where $\mathbf{W}_{r}$ is the $N \times N$ diagonal matrix with diagonal elements $\left(w_{r}[0], w_{r}[1], \ldots, w_{r}[N-1]\right)$.

In the next section, we will explore the implementation of the STFT in MATLAB, and discuss its applications in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1c Wavelet Transform

The Wavelet Transform is another powerful tool in time-frequency analysis, particularly useful in acoustical oceanography. It allows us to analyze the spectral content of a signal over time, but unlike the STFT, it does not require the signal to be divided into segments. This makes it particularly useful for signals that do not have a fixed frequency content, such as those found in the ocean.

The Wavelet Transform is computed using a wavelet function, which is a mathematical function that describes the shape of a wave. The wavelet function is convolved with the signal of interest, and the result is a two-dimensional plot where the horizontal axis represents time and the vertical axis represents frequency. The intensity of the plot at each point represents the power of the signal at that particular time and frequency.

The Wavelet Transform is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography as it allows us to visualize the changes in the spectral content of acoustic signals over time. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and spectral variations due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

The Wavelet Transform can be computed using the following formula:

$$
X[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}
$$

where $f[n]$ is the signal of interest, $w[n]$ is a wavelet function, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The Wavelet Transform can also be computed using the sliding window interpretation, which involves using the discrete Fourier transform (DFT). Let $w_r[n]=w[rL-n]$ be the window element obtained from shifted and flipped window $\mathbf{w}$. Then we have

$$
\mathbf{Y}=[\mathbf{Y}_0,\mathbf{Y}_1...,\mathbf{Y}_{R-1}]
$$

where $\mathbf{Y}_r = \mathbf{x}\circ\mathbf{w}_r$.

The Wavelet Transform phase retrieval problem can be stated as: Find $\mathbf{x}$ such that $
\mathbf{Z}_{w}[m,r]=|Y[m,r]|^2
$ where $\mathbf{W}_{r}$ is the $N \times N$ diagonal matrix with diagonal elements $\left(w_{r}[0], w_{r}[1], \ldots, w_{r}[N-1]\right)$.

In the next section, we will explore the implementation of the Wavelet Transform in MATLAB, and discuss its applications in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1d Applications of Time-Frequency Analysis

Time-frequency analysis, particularly the Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT) and Wavelet Transform, has a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. These techniques are used to analyze the spectral content of acoustic signals over time, providing valuable insights into the behavior of sound waves in the ocean.

One of the primary applications of time-frequency analysis in acoustical oceanography is in the study of ocean acoustics. The ocean is a complex medium, with its properties such as water depth, temperature, and salinity varying significantly. These variations can cause the spectral content of acoustic signals to change rapidly over time. Time-frequency analysis allows us to visualize these changes, providing a deeper understanding of how sound propagates in the ocean.

Another important application of time-frequency analysis is in the detection and classification of marine mammals. Marine mammals, such as whales and dolphins, communicate using acoustic signals. These signals can be analyzed using time-frequency techniques to identify the species of the mammal and its location. This information is crucial for monitoring and protecting these endangered species.

Time-frequency analysis is also used in the design and testing of underwater acoustic devices. By analyzing the spectral content of the signals emitted by these devices, engineers can optimize their performance and ensure that they meet the required specifications.

In addition to these applications, time-frequency analysis is also used in the study of ocean currents, the detection of underwater objects, and the analysis of ocean noise. The versatility of these techniques makes them an essential tool in the field of acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.1c Wavelet Transform

The Wavelet Transform is a mathematical tool that allows us to analyze the time-varying frequency content of a signal. This is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography, where the frequency content of acoustic signals can change rapidly over time due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

The Wavelet Transform is computed by dividing the signal into short segments, and applying a Wavelet Transform to each segment. The result is a two-dimensional plot where the horizontal axis represents time and the vertical axis represents frequency. The intensity of the plot at each point represents the power of the signal at that particular time and frequency.

The Wavelet Transform is particularly useful in acoustical oceanography as it allows us to visualize the changes in the frequency content of acoustic signals over time. This can be particularly useful in understanding the behavior of sound waves in the ocean, which can exhibit both temporal and spectral variations due to factors such as water depth, temperature, and salinity.

The Wavelet Transform can be computed using the following formula:

$$
X[m,r]=\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}{f[n]w[rL-n]e^{-i2\pi \frac{mn}{N}}}

where $f[n]$ is the signal of interest, $w[n]$ is a window function, $L$ is the separation in time between adjacent short-time sections, and $R = \left \lceil \frac{N+W-1}{L} \right \rceil$ is the number of short-time sections considered.

The Wavelet Transform can also be computed using the sliding window interpretation, which involves using the discrete Fourier transform (DFT). Let $w_r[n]=w[rL-n]$ be the window element obtained from shifted and flipped window $\mathbf{w}$. Then we have

\mathbf{Y}=[\mathbf{Y}_0,\mathbf{Y}_1...,\mathbf{Y}_{R-1}]

where $\mathbf{Y}_r = \mathbf{x}\circ\mathbf{w}_r$.

#### 4.2a Beamforming Techniques

Beamforming is a signal processing technique used in acoustical oceanography to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of received signals. It involves combining the signals received by multiple sensors to form a directional beam. This beam can then be steered in a specific direction to focus on the desired signal and suppress noise from other directions.

There are several beamforming techniques used in acoustical oceanography, each with its own advantages and limitations. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used beamforming techniques.

##### Delay and Sum Beamforming

Delay and Sum Beamforming is a time domain approach that is simple to implement. It works by delaying the signals received by each sensor by a different amount, and then summing the delayed signals. The amount of delay is determined by the distance between the sensors and the source of the signal. This technique can poorly estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal, but this can be improved by using a frequency domain approach.

##### Frequency Domain Beamforming

Frequency Domain Beamforming uses the Fourier transform to convert the time domain signals into the frequency domain. This allows the time delay between adjacent sensors to be represented as a phase shift. The array output vector at any time "t" can be denoted as $\boldsymbol x(t) = x_1(t)\begin{bmatrix} 1 & e^{-j\omega\Delta t} & \cdots & e^{-j\omega(M-1)\Delta t} \end{bmatrix}^T$, where $x_1(t)$ stands for the signal received by the first sensor.

The spatial covariance matrix, represented by $\boldsymbol R=E\{ \boldsymbol x(t) \boldsymbol x^T(t)\}$, carries the spatial and spectral information of the incoming signals. Assuming zero-mean Gaussian white noise, the basic model of the spatial covariance matrix is given by

$$
\boldsymbol R = \boldsymbol V \boldsymbol S \boldsymbol V^H + \sigma^2 \boldsymbol I \ \ (4)
$$

where $\sigma^2$ is the variance of the white noise, $\boldsymbol I$ is the identity matrix and $\boldsymbol V$ is the array manifold vector $\boldsymbol V = \begin{bmatrix} \boldsymbol v_1 & \cdots & \boldsymbol v_k \end{bmatrix}^T$ with $\boldsymbol v_i = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & e^{-j\omega\Delta t_i} & \cdots & e^{-j\omega(M-1)\Delta t_i} \end{bmatrix}^T$. This model is of central importance in frequency domain beamforming algorithms.

##### Conventional (Bartlett) Beamformer

The Bartlett Beamformer is a natural extension of conventional spectral analysis (spectrogram) to the sensor array. Its spectral power is represented by

$$
\hat{P}_{Bartlett}(\theta)=\boldsymbol v^H \boldsymbol R \boldsymbol v \ \ (5)
$$

The Bartlett Beamformer is particularly useful in situations where the signal of interest is stationary or slowly varying. However, it can be less effective in situations where the signal is rapidly varying or contains strong non-stationary components.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the challenges and limitations of beamforming in acoustical oceanography.

#### 4.2b Beamforming Algorithms

Beamforming algorithms are used to process the signals received by the sensors in a sensor array. These algorithms are designed to enhance the signal-to-noise ratio of the received signals, and to steer the beam in a specific direction. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used beamforming algorithms.

##### Least Variance Beamforming

The Least Variance Beamforming (LVB) algorithm is a frequency domain beamforming algorithm that minimizes the variance of the beamformer output. The LVB algorithm is based on the assumption that the noise is white and Gaussian. The LVB algorithm can be represented as:

$$
\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\text{LVB}} = \frac{\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}{\mathbf{d}^{\ast}\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}
$$

where $\mathbf{R}$ is the spatial covariance matrix, $\mathbf{d}$ is the desired signal vector, and $\mathbf{w}$ is the beamforming vector. The LVB algorithm can be used to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal, and to steer the beam in the direction of the estimated DOA.

##### MUSIC Beamforming

The MUSIC (MUltiple SIgnal Classification) Beamforming algorithm is a frequency domain beamforming algorithm that uses the eigenvalues of the spatial covariance matrix to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal. The MUSIC algorithm can be represented as:

$$
\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\text{MUSIC}} = \frac{\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}{\sqrt{\mathbf{d}^{\ast}\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}}
$$

where $\mathbf{R}$ is the spatial covariance matrix, $\mathbf{d}$ is the desired signal vector, and $\mathbf{w}$ is the beamforming vector. The MUSIC algorithm can be used to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal, and to steer the beam in the direction of the estimated DOA.

##### ESPRIT Beamforming

The ESPRIT (Estimation of Signal Parameters via Rotational Invariance Techniques) Beamforming algorithm is a frequency domain beamforming algorithm that uses the eigenvalues of the spatial covariance matrix to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal. The ESPRIT algorithm can be represented as:

$$
\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\text{ESPRIT}} = \frac{\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}{\sqrt{\mathbf{d}^{\ast}\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}}
$$

where $\mathbf{R}$ is the spatial covariance matrix, $\mathbf{d}$ is the desired signal vector, and $\mathbf{w}$ is the beamforming vector. The ESPRIT algorithm can be used to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal, and to steer the beam in the direction of the estimated DOA.

##### Beamforming with Constraints

In some applications, it may be desirable to impose constraints on the beamforming vector. For example, the beamforming vector may be required to have a certain magnitude or phase. In such cases, the beamforming algorithm can be modified to incorporate these constraints. For example, the LVB algorithm can be modified to include a constraint on the magnitude of the beamforming vector, as follows:

$$
\hat{\mathbf{w}}_{\text{LVB}} = \frac{\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}{\mathbf{d}^{\ast}\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{d}}
$$

where $\mathbf{R}$ is the spatial covariance matrix, $\mathbf{d}$ is the desired signal vector, and $\mathbf{w}$ is the beamforming vector. The LVB algorithm can be used to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of the signal, and to steer the beam in the direction of the estimated DOA.

#### 4.2c Beamforming Applications

Beamforming techniques have a wide range of applications in acoustical oceanography. These applications include, but are not limited to, the following:

##### Sonar Systems

Sonar systems are a common application of beamforming techniques. Sonar systems use sound waves to detect objects underwater. Beamforming allows these systems to focus on a specific direction, improving the signal-to-noise ratio and increasing the range at which objects can be detected.

##### Underwater Acoustics

Underwater acoustics is another important application of beamforming techniques. Underwater acoustics involves the study of sound propagation in water. Beamforming can be used to steer the beam in a specific direction, allowing for the study of sound propagation in a particular direction.

##### Oceanographic Research

Beamforming techniques are also used in oceanographic research. For example, beamforming can be used to estimate the direction of arrival (DOA) of a signal, which can be useful in studying the propagation of sound waves in the ocean.

##### Underwater Imaging

Underwater imaging is a technique used to create images of objects underwater. Beamforming can be used to focus the beam in a specific direction, improving the resolution of the image.

##### Underwater Communication

Underwater communication is a technique used to transmit information underwater. Beamforming can be used to focus the beam in a specific direction, improving the reliability of the communication.

In conclusion, beamforming techniques play a crucial role in acoustical oceanography, enabling researchers to study and understand the complex dynamics of sound propagation in the ocean.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of acoustical oceanography, exploring the depths of sound in the ocean. We have learned about the unique properties of sound in water, how it behaves, and how it can be used to study the ocean. We have also explored the various techniques and technologies used in acoustical oceanography, including signal processing, sonar systems, and underwater acoustics.

We have seen how acoustical oceanography plays a crucial role in understanding the ocean, from mapping the seafloor to studying marine life and ocean currents. We have also learned about the challenges and limitations of acoustical oceanography, and how researchers are constantly working to overcome these obstacles.

As we continue to explore the depths of sound in the ocean, we can look forward to exciting developments in acoustical oceanography. With advancements in technology and signal processing techniques, we can expect to gain even deeper insights into the ocean, and to better understand the complex dynamics of sound in this vast and mysterious environment.
