## Chapter 5: Acoustic Imaging:




### Introduction

The ocean is a vast and complex environment, teeming with life and filled with a multitude of sounds. From the gentle lapping of waves on the shore to the roar of ocean currents, the ocean is a symphony of sound. However, there is another layer of sound that permeates the ocean, one that is often overlooked but plays a crucial role in the marine ecosystem. This layer is known as ocean ambient noise.

In this chapter, we will delve into the world of ocean ambient noise, exploring its origins, characteristics, and impact on the marine environment. We will also discuss the various methods and technologies used to study and measure this noise, and the challenges faced in doing so.

Ocean ambient noise is a complex and dynamic phenomenon, influenced by a variety of factors including ocean currents, wind, and marine life. It is a critical component of the acoustic environment of the ocean, and understanding it is essential for a comprehensive understanding of the ocean's acoustics.

As we journey through this chapter, we will also touch upon the role of ocean ambient noise in various fields such as marine navigation, communication, and research. We will also explore the potential implications of human activities on ocean ambient noise, and the need for sustainable practices to protect this vital resource.

Join us as we explore the depths of sound in the ocean, and uncover the fascinating world of ocean ambient noise.




### Section: 5.1 Noise Sources:

Ocean ambient noise is a complex and dynamic phenomenon, influenced by a variety of factors including ocean currents, wind, and marine life. In this section, we will explore the various sources of ocean ambient noise, including shipping noise.

#### 5.1a Shipping Noise

Shipping is a vital part of global trade, with millions of tons of goods being transported across the world's oceans every year. However, this activity also generates a significant amount of noise, which can have a profound impact on the marine environment.

The noise generated by shipping can be broadly categorized into two types: mechanical noise and electrical noise. Mechanical noise is generated by the movement of the ship, including the engines, propellers, and hull. Electrical noise, on the other hand, is generated by the ship's electrical systems, including the navigation equipment and communication systems.

The noise generated by shipping can be quantified using various metrics, including the sound pressure level (SPL) and the noise-to-carry ratio (NC). The SPL is a measure of the loudness of a sound, while the NC is a measure of the noise relative to the carrier signal.

The impact of shipping noise on the marine environment is a topic of ongoing research. Studies have shown that shipping noise can disrupt the communication and navigation of marine animals, leading to changes in their behavior and potentially affecting their survival. Furthermore, shipping noise can also interfere with the detection of acoustic signals used in underwater communication and navigation, posing a safety risk for ships.

To mitigate the impact of shipping noise, various measures have been proposed, including the use of quieter engines and hull designs, as well as the implementation of noise-reducing technologies. Additionally, regulations have been put in place to limit the noise generated by ships, particularly in sensitive marine areas.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: marine life.

#### 5.1b Marine Life Noise

Marine life is another significant source of ocean ambient noise. The sounds produced by marine life are diverse and complex, ranging from the low-frequency rumblings of whales to the high-frequency clicks of dolphins. These sounds are used for communication, navigation, and hunting, and they play a crucial role in the marine ecosystem.

The study of marine life noise is a rapidly growing field known as bioacoustics. Bioacoustics researchers use a variety of techniques to study marine life noise, including hydrophones, which are underwater microphones, and acoustic tags, which are devices that can be attached to marine animals to track their movements and record their sounds.

One of the most intriguing aspects of marine life noise is its potential for communication. Many marine animals, including dolphins, whales, and fish, use sound to communicate over long distances. These sounds can travel hundreds of kilometers and can be used for a variety of purposes, including the location of prey, the identification of individuals, and the coordination of group activities.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. For example, the use of sonar by military vessels and commercial ships can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, marine life noise can also be used for navigation. Many marine animals, including dolphins and turtles, use sound to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of marine life noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds produced by marine life, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of these populations, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: ocean currents.

#### 5.1c Ocean Current Noise

Ocean currents are another significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of water in the ocean creates a variety of sounds, ranging from the gentle lapping of waves to the roar of ocean currents. These sounds are not only a part of the ocean's acoustic environment, but they also play a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and marine life.

The study of ocean current noise is a complex field that combines elements of acoustics, oceanography, and physics. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study ocean current noise, including hydrophones, acoustic tomography, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of ocean current noise is its potential for communication. The movement of water in the ocean can create sounds that can travel long distances and can be used for communication by marine animals. For example, some species of fish use the vibrations created by ocean currents to communicate over long distances.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. The movement of water can create sounds that can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, ocean current noise can also be used for navigation. The movement of water can create sounds that can be used by marine animals to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of ocean current noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds created by ocean currents, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: wind.

#### 5.1d Wind Noise

Wind is another significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of air over the surface of the ocean creates a variety of sounds, ranging from the gentle rustling of waves to the roar of wind. These sounds are not only a part of the ocean's acoustic environment, but they also play a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and marine life.

The study of wind noise is a complex field that combines elements of acoustics, meteorology, and oceanography. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study wind noise, including microphones, wind tunnels, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of wind noise is its potential for communication. The movement of air can create sounds that can travel long distances and can be used for communication by marine animals. For example, some species of birds use the rustling of wind to communicate over long distances.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. The movement of air can create sounds that can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, wind noise can also be used for navigation. The movement of air can create sounds that can be used by marine animals to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of wind noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds created by wind, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: waves.

#### 5.1e Wave Noise

Waves are a fundamental part of the ocean's acoustic environment, and they are a significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of water creates a variety of sounds, ranging from the gentle lapping of waves to the roar of ocean waves. These sounds are not only a part of the ocean's acoustic environment, but they also play a crucial role in the transport of heat, nutrients, and marine life.

The study of wave noise is a complex field that combines elements of acoustics, oceanography, and physics. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study wave noise, including hydrophones, acoustic tomography, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of wave noise is its potential for communication. By studying the sounds created by waves, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: ocean floor noise.

#### 5.1f Ocean Floor Noise

The ocean floor is a vast and complex surface, and it is a significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of water over the ocean floor, as well as the interaction between the ocean floor and the overlying water, creates a variety of sounds that are integral to the ocean's acoustic environment.

The study of ocean floor noise is a multidisciplinary field that combines elements of acoustics, geology, and oceanography. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study ocean floor noise, including hydrophones, seismic instruments, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of ocean floor noise is its potential for communication. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can travel long distances and can be used for communication by marine animals. For example, some species of fish use the vibrations created by the movement of water over the ocean floor to communicate over long distances.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, ocean floor noise can also be used for navigation. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can be used by marine animals to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of ocean floor noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds created by the ocean floor, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: ocean floor noise.

#### 5.1h Ocean Floor Noise

The ocean floor is a vast and complex surface, and it is a significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of water over the ocean floor, as well as the interaction between the ocean floor and the overlying water, creates a variety of sounds that are integral to the ocean's acoustic environment.

The study of ocean floor noise is a multidisciplinary field that combines elements of acoustics, geology, and oceanography. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study ocean floor noise, including hydrophones, seismic instruments, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of ocean floor noise is its potential for communication. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can travel long distances and can be used for communication by marine animals. For example, some species of fish use the vibrations created by the movement of water over the ocean floor to communicate over long distances.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, ocean floor noise can also be used for navigation. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can be used by marine animals to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of ocean floor noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds created by the ocean floor, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

In the next section, we will explore another significant source of ocean ambient noise: ocean floor noise.

#### 5.1i Ocean Floor Noise

The ocean floor is a vast and complex surface, and it is a significant source of ocean ambient noise. The movement of water over the ocean floor, as well as the interaction between the ocean floor and the overlying water, creates a variety of sounds that are integral to the ocean's acoustic environment.

The study of ocean floor noise is a multidisciplinary field that combines elements of acoustics, geology, and oceanography. Researchers use a variety of techniques to study ocean floor noise, including hydrophones, seismic instruments, and numerical models.

One of the most intriguing aspects of ocean floor noise is its potential for communication. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can travel long distances and can be used for communication by marine animals. For example, some species of fish use the vibrations created by the movement of water over the ocean floor to communicate over long distances.

However, the production of these sounds can also be a source of noise pollution. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can interfere with the communication of marine animals, potentially disrupting their behavior and affecting their survival.

In addition to communication, ocean floor noise can also be used for navigation. The movement of water over the ocean floor can create sounds that can be used by marine animals to navigate and orient themselves in the water. This is particularly important in the deep sea, where visual cues are limited.

The study of ocean floor noise is not only important for understanding the behavior of marine animals, but also for managing their populations and protecting their habitats. By studying the sounds created by the ocean floor, researchers can gain insights into the health and behavior of marine ecosystems, and use this information to inform conservation efforts.

### Subsection: 5.1b Biological Noise

Biological noise is a significant component of ocean ambient noise. It is generated by various marine organisms, including marine mammals, fish, and invertebrates. The noise generated by these organisms can be categorized into two types: vocal noise and non-vocal noise.

#### 5.1b.1 Vocal Noise

Vocal noise is generated by marine mammals, such as dolphins, harbour porpoises, and harbour seals, as well as fish and invertebrates. This type of noise is used for communication, navigation, and defense. The characteristics of vocal noise can vary significantly depending on the species and the purpose of the communication.

For instance, the vocal noise generated by dolphins and porpoises is characterized by high-frequency, short-duration pulses. These pulses are used for echolocation, a process by which these animals use sound waves to navigate and detect prey. The vocal noise generated by fish and invertebrates, on the other hand, is typically lower in frequency and longer in duration. This noise is used for communication and defense, particularly in species that live in large groups.

#### 5.1b.2 Non-Vocal Noise

Non-vocal noise is generated by the movement of marine organisms through the water. This type of noise is typically lower in frequency and longer in duration compared to vocal noise. The characteristics of non-vocal noise can vary depending on the size and type of the organism.

For instance, the non-vocal noise generated by large marine mammals, such as whales, is characterized by low-frequency, long-duration sounds. These sounds are used for communication and navigation. The non-vocal noise generated by smaller organisms, such as fish and invertebrates, is typically higher in frequency and shorter in duration. This noise is used for propulsion and maneuvering.

The impact of biological noise on the marine environment is a topic of ongoing research. Studies have shown that biological noise can disrupt the communication and navigation of marine animals, leading to changes in their behavior and potentially affecting their survival. Furthermore, biological noise can also interfere with the detection of acoustic signals used in underwater communication and navigation, posing a safety risk for ships.

To mitigate the impact of biological noise, various measures have been proposed, including the use of quieter engines and hull designs, as well as the implementation of noise-reducing technologies. Additionally, regulations have been put in place to limit the noise generated by ships, particularly in sensitive marine areas.




### Subsection: 5.1c Geological Noise

Geological noise is a significant component of ocean ambient noise. It is generated by various geological processes, including ocean waves, tides, and seismic activity. The noise generated by these processes can be categorized into two types: continuous noise and transient noise.

#### 5.1c.1 Continuous Noise

Continuous noise is generated by ongoing geological processes, such as ocean waves and tides. Ocean waves, in particular, are a major source of continuous noise in the ocean. The characteristics of this noise can vary significantly depending on the type of ocean wave, the depth of the water, and the presence of underwater topography.

Ocean waves can be categorized into two types: wind-generated waves and tidal waves. Wind-generated waves are generated by the wind blowing over the surface of the ocean. These waves are characterized by high-frequency, short-duration pulses. Tidal waves, on the other hand, are generated by the gravitational pull of the moon and the sun. These waves are characterized by lower frequencies and longer durations.

The presence of underwater topography can also significantly impact the characteristics of ocean waves. For instance, the interaction of ocean waves with underwater ridges and valleys can lead to the generation of secondary waves with different frequencies and durations.

#### 5.1c.2 Transient Noise

Transient noise is generated by sudden geological events, such as earthquakes and underwater landslides. These events can generate high-amplitude, low-frequency noise that can last for extended periods. The characteristics of this noise can vary significantly depending on the type of event and the properties of the ocean floor.

Earthquakes, for instance, can generate noise with frequencies as low as 0.1 Hz and durations of several minutes. The noise generated by underwater landslides, on the other hand, can be characterized by high-amplitude, low-frequency noise that can last for hours.

The study of geological noise is crucial for understanding the dynamics of the ocean and its interaction with the Earth's crust. It can also provide valuable information about the properties of the ocean floor and the presence of underwater topography.




### Subsection: 5.1d Anthropogenic Noise

Anthropogenic noise, also known as human-generated noise, is a significant contributor to the overall ambient noise in the ocean. This noise is primarily generated by human activities such as shipping, offshore industries, and military operations.

#### 5.1d.1 Shipping Noise

Shipping is a major source of anthropogenic noise in the ocean. The International Maritime Organization estimates that there are over 100,000 merchant ships and 1,000 cruise ships in the world, and these vessels generate significant levels of noise. The noise generated by ships can be categorized into two types: continuous noise and transient noise.

Continuous noise is generated by the engines of the ships and can be heard over long distances. The characteristics of this noise can vary depending on the type of engine, the speed of the ship, and the weather conditions. Transient noise, on the other hand, is generated by sudden events such as collisions or equipment failures. These events can generate high-amplitude, low-frequency noise that can last for extended periods.

#### 5.1d.2 Noise from Offshore Industries

Offshore industries such as oil and gas exploration and production, wind farms, and wave energy converters also contribute significantly to anthropogenic noise in the ocean. These industries often use equipment such as seismic airguns, pile drivers, and pumps that generate high levels of noise.

Seismic airguns, for instance, are used to explore for oil and gas deposits. These guns emit high-intensity pulses of sound waves into the water, which can be heard over long distances. The noise generated by these guns can be harmful to marine life, particularly to cetaceans, which can suffer permanent hearing loss if exposed to sound levels above 230 dB.

Pile drivers and pumps are also significant sources of noise. Pile drivers are used to drive piles into the seafloor, and they generate high levels of noise that can be heard over long distances. Pumps, on the other hand, are used to move fluids such as oil and gas, and they can generate continuous noise that can be heard over long distances.

#### 5.1d.3 Noise from Military Operations

Military operations, particularly naval exercises and maneuvers, also contribute to anthropogenic noise in the ocean. These operations often involve the use of sonar systems, which emit high levels of sound waves into the water. The noise generated by these systems can be harmful to marine life, particularly to cetaceans, which can suffer permanent hearing loss if exposed to sound levels above 230 dB.

In addition to sonar systems, military operations also involve the use of explosives, which can generate high levels of noise. These explosions can cause significant damage to marine life and infrastructure, and they can also generate noise that can be heard over long distances.

In conclusion, anthropogenic noise is a significant contributor to the overall ambient noise in the ocean. It is generated by a variety of human activities, and it can have significant impacts on marine life and ecosystems. As human activities continue to expand into the ocean, it is crucial to find ways to mitigate the impacts of anthropogenic noise.




### Subsection: 5.2a Power Spectral Density

The power spectral density (PSD) is a fundamental concept in the study of acoustical oceanography. It is a mathematical function that describes how the power of a signal is distributed over the frequency spectrum. In the context of ocean ambient noise, the PSD provides a comprehensive view of the noise spectrum, allowing us to understand the relative contributions of different sources of noise.

#### 5.2a.1 Definition of Power Spectral Density

The power spectral density of a signal $x(t)$ is defined as the Fourier transform of its autocorrelation function $R_x(\tau)$. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
S_x(f) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} R_x(\tau) e^{-j2\pi f\tau} d\tau
$$

where $S_x(f)$ is the power spectral density, $R_x(\tau)$ is the autocorrelation function, $j$ is the imaginary unit, and $f$ is the frequency.

The PSD provides a measure of the power of the signal at each frequency. The power at a given frequency is proportional to the square of the amplitude of the signal at that frequency. Therefore, the PSD can be thought of as a measure of the "strength" of the signal at each frequency.

#### 5.2a.2 Power Spectral Density and Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, the PSD can be used to analyze the noise spectrum and identify the sources of noise. The PSD can be estimated from a time series of noise measurements, providing a snapshot of the noise spectrum at a particular time and location.

The PSD can also be used to identify the dominant frequencies in the noise spectrum. For example, if a particular frequency is significantly higher than the surrounding frequencies, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at that frequency.

#### 5.2a.3 Power Spectral Density and Anthropogenic Noise

Anthropogenic noise, as discussed in the previous section, is a significant contributor to the overall ambient noise in the ocean. The PSD can be used to analyze the impact of anthropogenic noise on the ocean's acoustic environment.

For instance, the PSD can be used to analyze the noise generated by offshore industries. The PSD can provide insights into the frequency spectrum of the noise generated by these industries, allowing us to understand the impact of this noise on marine life.

Similarly, the PSD can be used to analyze the noise generated by shipping. The PSD can provide insights into the frequency spectrum of the noise generated by ships, allowing us to understand the impact of this noise on marine life.

In conclusion, the power spectral density is a powerful tool in the study of acoustical oceanography. It provides a comprehensive view of the noise spectrum, allowing us to understand the relative contributions of different sources of noise. In the next section, we will explore another important concept in acoustical oceanography: the cross power spectral density.




### Subsection: 5.2b Octave Band Analysis

Octave band analysis is a method used in acoustics to analyze the frequency content of a signal. It involves dividing the frequency spectrum into octave bands, each spanning a range of frequencies that are an octave apart. This method is particularly useful in the study of ocean ambient noise, as it allows us to focus on specific frequency ranges and identify the sources of noise within those ranges.

#### 5.2b.1 Definition of Octave Band Analysis

Octave band analysis is a method of spectral analysis that divides the frequency spectrum into octave bands. An octave is a range of frequencies that are twice as high or low as the central frequency of the band. The central frequency of each band is a power of two, and the bandwidth is constant across all bands.

The octave band analysis is typically represented as a function $H(f)$, where $f$ is the frequency and $H(f)$ is the amplitude of the signal at that frequency. The function $H(f)$ is defined as:

$$
H(f) = \begin{cases}
A_0, & \text{if } f \in [f_0, f_0 + B_0] \\
A_1, & \text{if } f \in [f_1, f_1 + B_1] \\
\vdots & \vdots \\
A_n, & \text{if } f \in [f_n, f_n + B_n]
\end{cases}
$$

where $A_i$ is the amplitude of the signal at the center frequency $f_i$ of the $i$-th band, and $B_i$ is the bandwidth of the $i$-th band.

#### 5.2b.2 Octave Band Analysis and Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, octave band analysis can be used to identify the sources of noise within specific frequency ranges. For example, if a particular octave band is significantly louder than the surrounding bands, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies.

Octave band analysis can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the amplitude of the signal at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

#### 5.2b.3 Octave Band Analysis and Anthropogenic Noise

Anthropogenic noise, as discussed in the previous sections, is a significant contributor to the overall ambient noise in the ocean. Octave band analysis can be used to identify the frequencies at which anthropogenic noise is most prevalent. This can help us understand the impact of human activities on the ocean's acoustic environment and develop strategies to mitigate the effects of anthropogenic noise.

In the next section, we will discuss another method of spectral analysis, the fast Fourier transform, and its application in the study of ocean ambient noise.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of ocean ambient noise, exploring its depths and understanding its complexities. We have learned that ambient noise is not just a random collection of sounds, but a structured and organized phenomenon that is influenced by a variety of factors. From the natural sounds of the ocean, such as the roar of the waves and the songs of marine life, to the anthropogenic noise generated by human activities, we have seen how these sounds interact and interfere with each other, creating a rich and dynamic acoustic environment.

We have also learned about the importance of understanding and studying ambient noise in the ocean. It is not just a matter of curiosity, but a crucial aspect of oceanography and marine biology. Ambient noise can provide valuable information about the state of the ocean, its inhabitants, and the impact of human activities. It can also be used as a tool for navigation and communication, and for monitoring and managing marine resources.

In conclusion, the study of ocean ambient noise is a vast and complex field, but one that is essential for understanding and managing our oceans. As we continue to explore and exploit the ocean, it is crucial that we do so in a way that minimizes the impact on its acoustic environment. By studying and understanding ambient noise, we can ensure that our activities do not disrupt the delicate balance of the ocean's acoustic ecosystem.

We have learned that ambient noise is not just a random collection of sounds, but a structured and organized phenomenon that is influenced by a variety of factors. From the natural sounds of the ocean, such as the roar of the waves and the songs of marine life, to the anthropogenic noise generated by human activities, we have seen how these sounds interact and interfere with each other, creating a rich and dynamic acoustic environment.

We have also learned about the importance of understanding and studying ambient noise in the ocean. It is not just a matter of curiosity, but a crucial aspect of oceanography and marine biology. By studying and understanding ambient noise, we can ensure that our activities do not disrupt the delicate balance of the ocean's acoustic ecosystem.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Ocean Noise Reduction

### Introduction

The ocean, with its vast expanse and complex ecosystem, is a critical environment for a multitude of species. However, the increasing levels of noise pollution in the ocean, primarily caused by human activities, have been a cause of concern for marine biologists and environmentalists. This chapter, "Ocean Noise Reduction," delves into the intricacies of this issue, exploring the sources of ocean noise, its impact on marine life, and the strategies for reducing it.

The ocean is not just a silent underwater world. It is teeming with sounds, from the gentle lapping of waves to the roar of ocean currents. However, the human-induced noise, such as the noise from ships, oil rigs, and sonar systems, has been significantly altering this natural acoustic environment. This noise not only disrupts the communication and behavior of marine animals but also poses a threat to their survival.

In this chapter, we will explore the various sources of ocean noise, including anthropogenic noise and natural noise. We will also delve into the impact of this noise on marine life, from the smallest organisms to the largest mammals. The chapter will also discuss the strategies for reducing ocean noise, including technological advancements and policy changes.

The study of ocean noise reduction is a multidisciplinary field, involving acoustics, marine biology, and environmental science. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of this field, equipping readers with the knowledge and understanding necessary to appreciate the complexity of the issue and contribute to its solution.

As we delve into the depths of ocean noise reduction, we will also explore the broader implications of this issue. The ocean is not just a source of food and resources; it is a vital part of our planet's ecosystem. The health of the ocean, and by extension, the health of the planet, is inextricably linked to the health of its acoustic environment. This chapter will provide a deeper understanding of this critical issue, paving the way for more informed and effective action.




### Subsection: 5.2c Statistical Characterization

Statistical characterization is a method used in acoustics to describe the statistical properties of a signal. In the context of ocean ambient noise, statistical characterization can provide valuable insights into the sources and behavior of noise in the ocean.

#### 5.2c.1 Definition of Statistical Characterization

Statistical characterization is a method of describing the statistical properties of a signal. It involves analyzing the distribution of the signal's values over time or frequency, and using statistical measures to describe this distribution.

The statistical characterization of a signal can be represented as a function $S(x)$, where $x$ is a value of the signal and $S(x)$ is the probability density function of the signal. The function $S(x)$ is defined as:

$$
S(x) = \frac{1}{N} \sum_{i=1}^{N} \delta(x - x_i)
$$

where $N$ is the number of samples of the signal, and $x_i$ is the $i$-th sample of the signal.

#### 5.2c.2 Statistical Characterization and Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, statistical characterization can be used to identify the sources of noise within specific frequency ranges. For example, if a particular frequency range has a high probability density, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies.

Statistical characterization can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the probability density functions at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

#### 5.2c.3 Statistical Characterization and Ocean Ambient Noise

Statistical characterization can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the probability density functions at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

### Subsection: 5.3a Hydrophone Calibration

Hydrophone calibration is a critical step in the measurement of ocean ambient noise. It involves the process of determining the relationship between the output voltage of a hydrophone and the pressure of the water. This calibration is necessary to ensure accurate and reliable measurements of ocean ambient noise.

#### 5.3a.1 Definition of Hydrophone Calibration

Hydrophone calibration is the process of determining the relationship between the output voltage of a hydrophone and the pressure of the water. This relationship is typically represented as a calibration curve, which plots the output voltage of the hydrophone against the pressure of the water.

The calibration curve is typically linear, but it can also be non-linear due to factors such as temperature changes or mechanical stress on the hydrophone. The calibration curve is used to correct the output voltage of the hydrophone to account for these non-linearities.

#### 5.3a.2 Hydrophone Calibration and Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, hydrophone calibration is crucial for accurate measurements. The calibration curve can be used to correct the output voltage of the hydrophone to account for the non-linearities introduced by factors such as temperature changes and mechanical stress.

The calibration curve can also be used to identify any changes in the sensitivity of the hydrophone over time. If the calibration curve changes significantly over time, it may indicate that the hydrophone is no longer functioning properly and should be replaced.

#### 5.3a.3 Hydrophone Calibration and Ocean Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, hydrophone calibration can be used to identify the sources of noise within specific frequency ranges. For example, if a particular frequency range has a high probability density, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies.

Hydrophone calibration can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the calibration curves at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

#### 5.3a.4 Hydrophone Calibration Techniques

There are several techniques for hydrophone calibration. One common technique is the use of a reference pressure standard, which is a device that produces a known pressure at a known frequency. The hydrophone is then calibrated by comparing its output voltage to the output voltage of the reference pressure standard.

Another technique is the use of a calibration tank, which is a controlled environment where the hydrophone can be exposed to a known pressure at a known frequency. The hydrophone is then calibrated by comparing its output voltage to the known pressure and frequency.

In the next section, we will discuss another important technique for measuring ocean ambient noise: the use of acoustic tomography.

### Subsection: 5.3b Noise Measurement Techniques

Noise measurement techniques are essential for accurately quantifying the ambient noise in the ocean. These techniques involve the use of various instruments and methods to capture and analyze the noise signals.

#### 5.3b.1 Noise Measurement Techniques and Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, noise measurement techniques are crucial for understanding the sources and characteristics of the noise. These techniques can provide valuable insights into the nature of the noise, its frequency content, and its spatial distribution.

Noise measurement techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: active and passive. Active techniques involve the use of active sources of sound, such as sonars or acoustic sources, to generate noise signals. Passive techniques, on the other hand, rely on the detection of noise signals generated by natural sources, such as marine life or ocean currents.

#### 5.3b.2 Noise Measurement Techniques and Ocean Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, noise measurement techniques can be used to identify the sources of noise within specific frequency ranges. For example, if a particular frequency range has a high probability density, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies.

Noise measurement techniques can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the noise spectra at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

For example, if a particular frequency range has a high probability density, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies.

Noise measurement techniques can also be used to compare the noise spectra at different times and locations. By comparing the noise spectra at the same frequencies, we can identify changes in the noise spectrum over time and across different regions of the ocean.

### Subsection: 5.3b Ambient Noise Recording

Ambient noise recording is a crucial aspect of ocean acoustics. It involves the process of capturing and recording the ambient noise present in the ocean. This recording can then be analyzed to understand the sources of noise, their characteristics, and their impact on marine life.

#### 5.3b.1 Definition of Ambient Noise Recording

Ambient noise recording is the process of capturing and recording the ambient noise present in the ocean. This noise can be generated by various sources, both natural and anthropogenic. The recording can be made using hydrophones, which are underwater microphones that can detect and record sound waves.

The recorded ambient noise can then be analyzed to identify the sources of noise, their characteristics, and their impact on marine life. This analysis can provide valuable insights into the health of the ocean and the effectiveness of noise mitigation efforts.

#### 5.3b.2 Ambient Noise Recording and Ocean Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, ambient noise recording is a crucial tool for understanding the sources of noise and their impact on marine life. The recorded ambient noise can be analyzed to identify the sources of noise, their characteristics, and their impact on marine life.

For example, if a particular frequency range has a high probability density, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies. This information can be used to identify the source of the noise and develop strategies to mitigate it.

#### 5.3b.3 Ambient Noise Recording and Ocean Ambient Noise

Ambient noise recording can also be used to monitor changes in the ambient noise over time. This can provide valuable insights into the effectiveness of noise mitigation efforts and the impact of anthropogenic activities on the ocean.

For example, if the recorded ambient noise shows a significant increase in a particular frequency range, it may indicate the presence of a new source of noise or an increase in the activity of an existing source. This information can be used to develop more effective noise mitigation strategies.

In conclusion, ambient noise recording is a crucial aspect of ocean acoustics. It provides a means to understand the sources of noise, their characteristics, and their impact on marine life. It also allows for the monitoring of changes in the ambient noise over time, providing valuable insights into the effectiveness of noise mitigation efforts.




### Subsection: 5.3c Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation

The Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR) is a critical parameter in the analysis of ambient noise recordings. It provides a measure of the quality of a signal, defined as the ratio of the power of the signal to the power of the noise. The SNR is usually expressed in decibels (dB).

#### 5.3c.1 Definition of Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation

Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation is the process of determining the SNR of a signal. This is typically done by measuring the power of the signal and the power of the noise, and then calculating the ratio of these two powers.

The power of a signal can be measured using a spectrum analyzer, which can provide a visual representation of the power of the signal at different frequencies. The power of the noise can be measured using the same method, or by subtracting the power of the signal from the total power of the recording.

#### 5.3c.2 Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation and Ocean Ambient Noise

In the context of ocean ambient noise, the SNR can provide valuable insights into the sources of noise and their impact on marine life. For example, a high SNR may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise, such as a ship or a marine mammal. Conversely, a low SNR may indicate the presence of a weak source of noise, such as a distant ship or a small marine mammal.

The SNR can also be used to monitor changes in the ambient noise over time. This can provide valuable insights into the effectiveness of noise mitigation efforts and the impact of anthropogenic activities on the ocean.

#### 5.3c.3 Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation and Ocean Ambient Noise

Signal-to-Noise Ratio Estimation can also be used to identify the sources of noise in the ocean. For example, if the SNR is high at a particular frequency, it may indicate the presence of a strong source of noise at those frequencies. This information can be used to identify the source of the noise and develop strategies to mitigate it.

In addition, the SNR can be used to monitor changes in the ambient noise over time. This can provide valuable insights into the effectiveness of noise mitigation efforts and the impact of anthropogenic activities on the ocean.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of ocean ambient noise. We have learned that ambient noise is the background noise present in the ocean, which is not generated by any specific source. It is a complex mixture of sounds from various sources, including wind, waves, and marine life. We have also discussed the importance of understanding and studying ambient noise in the ocean, as it can provide valuable insights into the health and functioning of the marine ecosystem.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the role of ambient noise in masking other sounds in the ocean. As we have seen, ambient noise can make it challenging to detect and interpret other sounds, such as those generated by marine mammals or human activities. This can have significant implications for our understanding of marine life and their behavior.

Furthermore, we have also discussed the impact of anthropogenic activities on ambient noise levels in the ocean. The increasing levels of noise pollution from human activities, such as shipping and offshore construction, can have detrimental effects on marine life and ecosystems. It is crucial for us to continue studying and monitoring ambient noise levels to better understand and mitigate these impacts.

In conclusion, ocean ambient noise is a complex and dynamic phenomenon that plays a crucial role in the marine environment. By studying and understanding ambient noise, we can gain valuable insights into the health and functioning of the ocean and its inhabitants. It is essential for us to continue exploring the depths of sound in the ocean to better understand and protect this vital ecosystem.

So let's dive in and explore the depths of sound in the ocean.




### Section: 6.1 Modulation Schemes:

Modulation is a crucial aspect of underwater acoustic communication, as it allows for the efficient transmission of information through the use of varying properties of a carrier signal. In this section, we will explore the different types of modulation schemes used in underwater acoustic communication, with a focus on amplitude shift keying (ASK).

#### 6.1a Amplitude Shift Keying (ASK)

Amplitude shift keying (ASK) is a type of digital modulation scheme that uses variations in the amplitude of a carrier signal to represent digital data. It is a form of amplitude modulation, where the amplitude of the carrier signal is varied to represent different symbols.

In ASK, a symbol, representing one or more bits, is sent by transmitting a fixed-amplitude carrier wave at a fixed frequency for a specific time duration. The amplitude of the carrier signal is then varied to represent different symbols. For example, if each symbol represents a single bit, then the carrier signal could be transmitted at nominal amplitude when the input value is 1, but transmitted at reduced amplitude or not at all when the input value is 0.

The demodulator, which is designed specifically for the symbol-set used by the modulator, determines the amplitude of the received signal and maps it back to the symbol it represents, thus recovering the original data. Frequency and phase of the carrier are kept constant.

ASK is a linear and sensitive to atmospheric noise, distortions, and propagation conditions. Both ASK modulation and demodulation processes are relatively inexpensive. The ASK technique is also commonly used to transmit digital data over optical fiber. For LED transmission, ASK is used because it is simple and inexpensive to implement.

In the next section, we will explore the advantages and disadvantages of ASK, as well as its applications in underwater acoustic communication.





### Section: 6.1 Modulation Schemes:

Modulation is a crucial aspect of underwater acoustic communication, as it allows for the efficient transmission of information through the use of varying properties of a carrier signal. In this section, we will explore the different types of modulation schemes used in underwater acoustic communication, with a focus on frequency shift keying (FSK).

#### 6.1b Frequency Shift Keying (FSK)

Frequency shift keying (FSK) is a type of digital modulation scheme that uses variations in the frequency of a carrier signal to represent digital data. It is a form of frequency modulation, where the frequency of the carrier signal is varied to represent different symbols.

In FSK, a symbol, representing one or more bits, is sent by transmitting a fixed-frequency carrier wave at a fixed amplitude for a specific time duration. The frequency of the carrier signal is then varied to represent different symbols. For example, if each symbol represents a single bit, then the carrier signal could be transmitted at a higher frequency when the input value is 1, but transmitted at a lower frequency when the input value is 0.

The demodulator, which is designed specifically for the symbol-set used by the modulator, determines the frequency of the received signal and maps it back to the symbol it represents, thus recovering the original data. Amplitude and phase of the carrier are kept constant.

FSK is a non-linear and less sensitive to atmospheric noise, distortions, and propagation conditions compared to ASK. However, it is more complex and expensive to implement. The FSK technique is also commonly used in wireless communication systems.

In the next section, we will explore the advantages and disadvantages of FSK, as well as its applications in underwater acoustic communication.





### Introduction to Underwater Acoustic Communication

Underwater acoustic communication is a crucial aspect of oceanography, allowing for the transmission of information and data in the underwater environment. This chapter will explore the various techniques and methods used for underwater acoustic communication, with a focus on modulation schemes.

Modulation is a fundamental concept in communication systems, where the information signal is modulated onto a carrier signal for transmission. In underwater acoustic communication, modulation is essential for efficient and reliable communication, as it allows for the transmission of information over long distances and through complex underwater environments.

In this chapter, we will delve into the different types of modulation schemes used in underwater acoustic communication, including frequency shift keying (FSK), phase shift keying (PSK), and amplitude shift keying (ASK). We will also explore the advantages and disadvantages of each scheme, as well as their applications in underwater acoustic communication.

Furthermore, we will discuss the challenges and limitations of underwater acoustic communication, such as signal propagation and interference, and how modulation schemes can help mitigate these issues. We will also touch upon the latest advancements and developments in underwater acoustic communication, such as cognitive radio and software-defined radio, and how they are shaping the future of this field.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of underwater acoustic communication, with a focus on modulation schemes, and their role in exploring the depths of sound in the ocean. 





#### 6.1d Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM)

Orthogonal Frequency Division Multiplexing (OFDM) is a digital modulation scheme that is widely used in underwater acoustic communication. It is a form of frequency-division multiplexing (FDM) where multiple signals are transmitted simultaneously over a single communication channel. OFDM is particularly useful in underwater acoustic communication due to its ability to achieve high data rates and its resistance to multipath fading.

##### Key Features of OFDM

The advantages and disadvantages of OFDM are further discussed in the Characteristics and principles of operation section below.

##### Characteristics and Principles of Operation

OFDM is a specialized form of FDM, with the additional constraint that all subcarrier signals within a communication channel are orthogonal to one another. This means that the subcarriers are carefully chosen so that they are orthogonal to each other, resulting in no crosstalk between the sub-channels. This greatly simplifies the design of both the transmitter and the receiver, as unlike conventional FDM, a separate filter for each sub-channel is not required.

The orthogonality of OFDM requires that the subcarrier spacing is $\Delta f = \frac{k}{T_U}$ Hertz, where $T_U$ seconds is the useful symbol duration (the receiver-side window size), and $k$ is a positive integer, typically equal to 1. This stipulates that each carrier frequency undergoes $k$ more complete cycles per symbol period than the previous carrier. Therefore, with $N$ subcarriers, the total passband bandwidth will be $B \approx N\Delta f$ (Hz).

The orthogonality also allows for high spectral efficiency, with a total symbol rate near the Nyquist rate for the equivalent baseband signal (i.e., near half the Nyquist rate for the double-side band physical passband signal). Almost the whole available frequency band can be used, resulting in a nearly 'white' spectrum, giving OFDM benign electromagnetic interference properties with respect to other co-channel users.

However, OFDM also requires very accurate frequency synchronization between the receiver and the transmitter. With frequency deviation, the subcarriers will no longer be orthogonal, causing inter-carrier interference (ICI) (i.e., cross-talk between the subcarriers). This can significantly degrade the performance of OFDM systems.

##### OFDM in Underwater Acoustic Communication

OFDM has been widely adopted in underwater acoustic communication due to its ability to achieve high data rates and its resistance to multipath fading. In underwater environments, signals can take multiple paths to reach the receiver, resulting in delayed and distorted versions of the transmitted signal. OFDM's use of multiple subcarriers allows for the detection and correction of these delayed and distorted signals, making it more robust to multipath fading than other modulation schemes.

Furthermore, OFDM's ability to achieve high data rates makes it suitable for applications that require large amounts of data to be transmitted, such as underwater imaging and mapping. Its high spectral efficiency also allows for efficient use of the limited bandwidth available in underwater communication.

In conclusion, OFDM is a powerful modulation scheme that is widely used in underwater acoustic communication. Its ability to achieve high data rates, its resistance to multipath fading, and its efficient use of bandwidth make it a valuable tool for exploring the depths of sound in the ocean. 





#### 6.2a Error Detection and Correction Codes

In the previous section, we discussed the use of Hamming codes for error detection and correction. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of error detection and correction codes, exploring their role in underwater acoustic communication.

##### Error Detection and Correction Codes

Error detection and correction codes are a crucial component of underwater acoustic communication. They are used to ensure the integrity of transmitted data, detecting and correcting errors that may occur during transmission. These codes are particularly important in underwater acoustics due to the challenging propagation conditions, which can lead to high error rates.

##### Types of Error Detection and Correction Codes

There are several types of error detection and correction codes, each with its own strengths and weaknesses. Some of the most commonly used types include Hamming codes, Reed-Solomon codes, and convolutional codes.

###### Hamming Codes

Hamming codes are a family of linear error-correcting codes. They are named after Richard Hamming, who first introduced them in 1950. Hamming codes are designed to detect and correct single-bit errors. They work by adding redundant parity bits to the transmitted data, which can be used to detect and correct single-bit errors.

###### Reed-Solomon Codes

Reed-Solomon codes are a family of cyclic error-correcting codes. They were first introduced by Irving S. Reed and Gustave Solomon in 1960. Reed-Solomon codes are particularly well-suited for correcting burst errors, making them ideal for use in underwater acoustic communication.

###### Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a family of linear error-correcting codes. They are particularly well-suited for correcting burst errors, making them ideal for use in underwater acoustic communication. Convolutional codes work by convolving the transmitted data with a set of generator polynomials, which can be used to detect and correct burst errors.

##### Error Detection and Correction Codes in Underwater Acoustic Communication

In underwater acoustic communication, error detection and correction codes are used to ensure the integrity of transmitted data. They are particularly important due to the challenging propagation conditions, which can lead to high error rates. The choice of error detection and correction code depends on the specific requirements of the communication system, including the type of errors that need to be detected and corrected, the complexity of the code, and the computational resources available.

In the next section, we will explore the use of error detection and correction codes in more detail, discussing their implementation and performance in underwater acoustic communication.

#### 6.2b Channel Capacity and Shannon's Theorem

In the previous section, we discussed the importance of error detection and correction codes in underwater acoustic communication. In this section, we will explore the concept of channel capacity and Shannon's theorem, which provide a theoretical limit on the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel.

##### Channel Capacity

Channel capacity, denoted by $C$, is a fundamental concept in information theory. It represents the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel. In the context of underwater acoustic communication, the channel is the medium through which the acoustic signals travel, and the noise is the interference caused by the propagation conditions.

The channel capacity is determined by the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR), which is the ratio of the power of the signal to the power of the noise. The higher the SNR, the higher the channel capacity. However, in underwater acoustics, the SNR is often low due to the challenging propagation conditions.

##### Shannon's Theorem

Shannon's theorem, named after Claude Shannon, provides a theoretical limit on the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel. It states that the maximum rate of reliable communication over a noisy channel is given by the channel capacity minus the entropy of the noise.

In the context of underwater acoustic communication, Shannon's theorem can be used to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel. This can be useful in designing communication systems, as it provides a theoretical limit on the performance of the system.

##### Implications for Underwater Acoustic Communication

The concept of channel capacity and Shannon's theorem has important implications for underwater acoustic communication. It provides a theoretical limit on the performance of communication systems, which can be used to guide the design of these systems. It also highlights the importance of error detection and correction codes, as they can help to increase the channel capacity by reducing the entropy of the noise.

In the next section, we will explore the practical implications of these concepts, discussing how they can be applied in the design of underwater acoustic communication systems.

#### 6.2c Modulation and Demodulation Techniques

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of channel capacity and Shannon's theorem, which provide a theoretical limit on the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a noisy channel. In this section, we will explore the practical aspects of underwater acoustic communication, focusing on modulation and demodulation techniques.

##### Modulation Techniques

Modulation is the process of varying one or more properties of a carrier signal to transmit information. In underwater acoustic communication, the carrier signal is the acoustic wave, and the properties that are varied are typically the amplitude, frequency, and phase of the wave.

There are several types of modulation techniques used in underwater acoustic communication, including amplitude modulation (AM), frequency modulation (FM), phase modulation (PM), and quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM). Each of these techniques has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of modulation technique depends on the specific requirements of the communication system.

##### Demodulation Techniques

Demodulation is the process of extracting the information from a modulated signal. In underwater acoustic communication, demodulation is typically performed by a receiver that is located near the transmitter.

The demodulation process involves converting the modulated signal back into its original form, which can then be decoded to recover the transmitted information. The choice of demodulation technique depends on the type of modulation used in the communication system.

##### Implications for Underwater Acoustic Communication

The choice of modulation and demodulation techniques has a significant impact on the performance of underwater acoustic communication systems. The choice of modulation technique affects the bandwidth of the transmitted signal, the power requirements, and the robustness of the signal against noise and interference. The choice of demodulation technique affects the complexity of the receiver, the sensitivity to synchronization errors, and the ability to decode the signal in the presence of noise and interference.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of modulation and demodulation techniques, and discuss their applications in underwater acoustic communication.

#### 6.2d Multiple Access Techniques

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concepts of modulation and demodulation techniques, which are fundamental to underwater acoustic communication. In this section, we will delve into the topic of multiple access techniques, which are essential for enabling multiple users to share the same communication channel.

##### Multiple Access Techniques

Multiple access techniques are used to allow multiple users to share the same communication channel. This is particularly important in underwater acoustic communication, where the available bandwidth is often limited and expensive.

There are several types of multiple access techniques used in underwater acoustic communication, including time division multiple access (TDMA), frequency division multiple access (FDMA), and code division multiple access (CDMA). Each of these techniques has its own advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of multiple access technique depends on the specific requirements of the communication system.

##### Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA)

In time division multiple access (TDMA), different users are assigned different time slots in a shared communication channel. Each user is allowed to transmit during their assigned time slot, and the channel is shared among the users in a time-division manner.

TDMA is simple to implement and provides good spectral efficiency. However, it requires a synchronous clock at each user, which can be difficult to achieve in practice.

##### Frequency Division Multiple Access (FDMA)

In frequency division multiple access (FDMA), different users are assigned different frequency bands in a shared communication channel. Each user is allowed to transmit in their assigned frequency band, and the channel is shared among the users in a frequency-division manner.

FDMA provides good spectral efficiency and is relatively easy to implement. However, it requires a wide bandwidth, which can be expensive.

##### Code Division Multiple Access (CDMA)

In code division multiple access (CDMA), different users are assigned different spreading codes, which are used to spread the transmitted signals over a wide frequency band. Each user's signal is then combined with the signals of other users in the receiver, and the signals are separated using the same spreading codes.

CDMA provides good spectral efficiency and is robust against interference. However, it requires complex signal processing at the receiver, which can be difficult to implement in practice.

##### Implications for Underwater Acoustic Communication

The choice of multiple access technique has a significant impact on the performance of underwater acoustic communication systems. The choice of multiple access technique affects the bandwidth requirements, the complexity of the receiver, the robustness against interference, and the scalability of the system.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of multiple access techniques, and discuss their applications in underwater acoustic communication.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of underwater acoustic communication. We have explored the unique characteristics of sound propagation in water, the challenges posed by the underwater environment, and the innovative solutions that have been developed to overcome these challenges. We have also examined the various applications of underwater acoustic communication, from scientific research to military operations, and the potential for future developments in this field.

The study of underwater acoustic communication is a complex and multidisciplinary field, requiring knowledge of acoustics, signal processing, electronics, and computer science. It is a field that is constantly evolving, with new technologies and techniques being developed to improve the performance and reliability of underwater acoustic communication systems.

As we have seen, underwater acoustic communication plays a crucial role in many areas of oceanography. It provides a means of communication between underwater vehicles and surface stations, enabling the collection and transmission of data in real-time. It also allows for the monitoring of underwater environments, the detection of marine life, and the tracking of underwater objects.

In conclusion, the study of underwater acoustic communication is a challenging but rewarding field, offering many opportunities for research and development. It is a field that is essential for our understanding of the ocean and its inhabitants, and for our ability to explore and exploit the resources of the underwater world.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Underwater Acoustics and Sonar

### Introduction

The underwater world is a vast and mysterious realm, teeming with life and filled with secrets waiting to be discovered. One of the most powerful tools for exploring this hidden world is sound. In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating field of underwater acoustics and sonar, exploring how sound is used to navigate, communicate, and study the ocean.

Underwater acoustics is the study of sound in water, and it plays a crucial role in many aspects of oceanography. From the propagation of sound waves to the detection of marine life, underwater acoustics provides a unique perspective on the ocean. Sonar, short for sound navigation and ranging, is a technology that uses sound waves to navigate and detect objects underwater. It is a vital tool for marine navigation and research.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of underwater acoustics and sonar, their applications in oceanography, and the latest advancements in this field. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of working with sound in the underwater environment, and how these are being addressed by researchers and engineers.

Whether you are a student, a researcher, or simply a curious reader, this chapter will provide you with a comprehensive understanding of underwater acoustics and sonar. It will equip you with the knowledge and tools to explore the underwater world in a whole new way. So, let's dive in and discover the wonders of underwater acoustics and sonar.




#### 6.2b Convolutional Codes

Convolutional codes are a type of error detection and correction code that are particularly well-suited for correcting burst errors. They are a family of linear error-correcting codes that work by convolving the transmitted data with a set of generator polynomials. This process allows them to detect and correct burst errors, making them ideal for use in underwater acoustic communication.

##### Convolutional Codes in Underwater Acoustic Communication

In underwater acoustic communication, the propagation conditions can be challenging, leading to high error rates. Convolutional codes are particularly useful in these conditions due to their ability to correct burst errors. This is because burst errors are common in underwater acoustics, where the signal can be disrupted by various factors such as water temperature, salinity, and pressure.

##### Convolutional Codes and the Viterbi Algorithm

The Viterbi algorithm is a dynamic programming algorithm used for finding the most likely sequence of hidden states in a hidden Markov model. In the context of convolutional codes, the Viterbi algorithm is used for decoding the transmitted data. The algorithm works by finding the most likely sequence of transmitted data given the received data.

##### Concatenation with Outer Algebraic Codes

One issue with convolutional codes is their susceptibility to error floors. An error floor is a phenomenon where the error rate does not decrease significantly even as the code rate is decreased. To address this issue, concatenation with an outer algebraic code, such as a Reed-Solomon code, can be used. This approach allows for the benefits of both types of codes, providing good performance at high code rates and also addressing the issue of error floors.

##### Implicit Data Structure

The implicit data structure is a concept that is closely related to convolutional codes. It is a data structure that is used to represent the convolutional code. The implicit data structure is particularly useful in the context of underwater acoustic communication, where the propagation conditions can be challenging and the use of efficient data structures is crucial.

##### Further Reading

For more information on convolutional codes and their applications in underwater acoustic communication, we recommend reading the publications of Hervé Brönnimann, J. Ian Munro, and Greg Frederickson. These authors have made significant contributions to the field and their work provides valuable insights into the use of convolutional codes in underwater acoustics.

#### 6.2c Applications of Channel Coding

Channel coding, particularly convolutional codes, have a wide range of applications in underwater acoustic communication. These applications extend beyond error detection and correction, and include the use of channel coding in distributed source coding and multi-focus image fusion.

##### Distributed Source Coding

Distributed source coding is a technique used in data compression where the source data is distributed among multiple nodes. This technique is particularly useful in underwater acoustic communication, where the propagation conditions can be challenging and the use of efficient data compression techniques is crucial. Convolutional codes can be used in distributed source coding to compress the source data, making it easier to transmit over the channel.

##### Multi-focus Image Fusion

Multi-focus image fusion is a technique used in image processing to combine multiple images of the same scene taken at different focus settings. This technique is particularly useful in underwater acoustic communication, where the propagation conditions can be challenging and the use of high-quality images is crucial. Convolutional codes can be used in multi-focus image fusion to compress the multiple images, making it easier to transmit them over the channel.

##### Further Reading

For more information on the applications of channel coding in underwater acoustic communication, we recommend reading the publications of Hervé Brönnimann, J. Ian Munro, and Greg Frederickson. These authors have made significant contributions to the field and their work provides valuable insights into the use of channel coding in distributed source coding and multi-focus image fusion.

#### 6.2d Future Directions in Channel Coding

As technology continues to advance, the field of channel coding is expected to evolve and expand. The development of new coding schemes and the improvement of existing ones will be crucial in meeting the increasing demand for reliable and efficient communication in underwater acoustics.

##### Turbo Codes

Turbo codes, a class of iterated short convolutional codes, are expected to play a significant role in the future of channel coding. These codes closely approach the theoretical limits imposed by Shannon's theorem with much less decoding complexity than the Viterbi algorithm on the long convolutional codes that would be required for the same performance. The concatenation with an outer algebraic code (e.g., Reed–Solomon) addresses the issue of error floors inherent to turbo code designs.

##### Implicit Data Structure

The concept of implicit data structure is expected to be further explored in the context of channel coding. The implicit data structure is a data structure that is used to represent the convolutional code. It is particularly useful in the context of underwater acoustic communication, where the propagation conditions can be challenging and the use of efficient data structures is crucial.

##### Further Reading

For more information on the future directions of channel coding, we recommend reading the publications of Hervé Brönnimann, J. Ian Munro, and Greg Frederickson. These authors have made significant contributions to the field and their work provides valuable insights into the future of channel coding.
