## Chapter 7: Underwater Acoustic Imaging

### Introduction

The ocean, with its vast expanse and mysterious depths, has always been a subject of fascination and exploration. The ability to see and understand what lies beneath the surface of the ocean is a crucial aspect of oceanography. This is where underwater acoustic imaging comes into play. In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of underwater acoustic imaging, exploring its principles, techniques, and applications.

Underwater acoustic imaging is a non-invasive technique that uses sound waves to create images of the underwater environment. This technique is particularly useful in the study of the ocean, where visibility is often limited due to the presence of particles and organisms in the water. By sending out sound waves and measuring the time it takes for them to bounce back, we can create a picture of the ocean floor, marine life, and other features.

The chapter will begin by introducing the basic principles of underwater acoustic imaging, including the generation and propagation of sound waves in the ocean. We will then explore the different types of underwater acoustic imaging techniques, such as single-beam and multi-beam imaging, and their respective advantages and disadvantages. 

Next, we will delve into the applications of underwater acoustic imaging in oceanography. This includes the mapping of the ocean floor, the study of marine life, and the detection of underwater objects. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of underwater acoustic imaging, and how these can be overcome.

Finally, we will touch upon the future of underwater acoustic imaging, discussing the latest advancements and future directions in this field. This includes the use of artificial intelligence and machine learning in underwater acoustic imaging, and the potential for underwater acoustic imaging to contribute to the sustainable management of the ocean.

In this chapter, we aim to provide a comprehensive overview of underwater acoustic imaging, from its basic principles to its advanced applications. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of oceanography, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of underwater acoustic imaging and its role in exploring the depths of sound in the ocean.




#### 6.2c Turbo Codes

Turbo codes are a type of error detection and correction code that are particularly well-suited for correcting burst errors. They are a family of linear error-correcting codes that work by convolving the transmitted data with a set of generator polynomials. This process allows them to detect and correct burst errors, making them ideal for use in underwater acoustic communication.

##### Turbo Codes in Underwater Acoustic Communication

In underwater acoustic communication, the propagation conditions can be challenging, leading to high error rates. Turbo codes are particularly useful in these conditions due to their ability to correct burst errors. This is because burst errors are common in underwater acoustics, where the signal can be disrupted by various factors such as water temperature, salinity, and pressure.

##### Turbo Codes and the BCJR Algorithm

The BCJR (Bahl, Cocke, Jelinek, and Raviv) algorithm is a dynamic programming algorithm used for finding the most likely sequence of hidden states in a hidden Markov model. In the context of turbo codes, the BCJR algorithm is used for decoding the transmitted data. The algorithm works by finding the most likely sequence of transmitted data given the received data.

##### Concatenation with Outer Algebraic Codes

One issue with turbo codes is their susceptibility to error floors. An error floor is a phenomenon where the error rate does not decrease significantly even as the code rate is decreased. To address this issue, concatenation with an outer algebraic code, such as a Reed-Solomon code, can be used. This approach allows for the benefits of both types of codes, providing good performance at high code rates and also addressing the issue of error floors.

##### Implicit Data Structure

The implicit data structure is a concept that is closely related to turbo codes. It is a data structure that is used to represent the turbo code. The implicit data structure is particularly useful in the context of underwater acoustic communication, where the propagation conditions can be challenging and the error rates can be high. The implicit data structure allows for efficient decoding of the transmitted data, even in the presence of high error rates.

##### Conclusion

In conclusion, turbo codes are a powerful tool for error detection and correction in underwater acoustic communication. Their ability to correct burst errors makes them particularly useful in the challenging propagation conditions found in underwater acoustics. The BCJR algorithm, concatenation with outer algebraic codes, and the implicit data structure all play important roles in the performance of turbo codes in underwater acoustic communication.




#### 6.2d Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) Codes

Low-Density Parity-Check (LDPC) codes are a family of linear error-correcting codes that are particularly well-suited for correcting random errors. They are named as such because they have a low density of parity-check constraints, which makes them computationally efficient to decode. LDPC codes have been widely adopted in various communication systems, including underwater acoustic communication.

##### LDPC Codes in Underwater Acoustic Communication

In underwater acoustic communication, the propagation conditions can be challenging, leading to high error rates. LDPC codes are particularly useful in these conditions due to their ability to correct random errors. This is because random errors are common in underwater acoustics, where the signal can be disrupted by various factors such as water temperature, salinity, and pressure.

##### LDPC Codes and the BCJR Algorithm

The BCJR (Bahl, Cocke, Jelinek, and Raviv) algorithm is a dynamic programming algorithm used for finding the most likely sequence of hidden states in a hidden Markov model. In the context of LDPC codes, the BCJR algorithm is used for decoding the transmitted data. The algorithm works by finding the most likely sequence of transmitted data given the received data.

##### Concatenation with Outer Algebraic Codes

One issue with LDPC codes is their susceptibility to error floors. An error floor is a phenomenon where the error rate does not decrease significantly even as the code rate is decreased. To address this issue, concatenation with an outer algebraic code, such as a Reed-Solomon code, can be used. This approach allows for the benefits of both types of codes, providing good performance at high code rates and also addressing the issue of error floors.

##### Implicit Data Structure

The implicit data structure is a concept that is closely related to LDPC codes. It is a data structure that is used to represent the LDPC code. The implicit data structure is particularly useful in the context of LDPC codes due to its ability to efficiently represent the code and facilitate decoding.

##### LDPC Codes and the BCHB Code

The BCHB (Bose-Chaudhuri-Hocquenghem-Blahut) code is a type of LDPC code that is particularly well-suited for correcting random errors. It is named as such because it is a combination of the BCH (Bose-Chaudhuri-Hocquenghem) code and the Blahut algorithm. The BCHB code is particularly useful in underwater acoustic communication due to its ability to correct random errors, which are common in these conditions.

##### LDPC Codes and the BCHB Code

The BCHB code is particularly useful in underwater acoustic communication due to its ability to correct random errors, which are common in these conditions.
